Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
The Major Maxim: Harm Must Be Removed and Its Scope
Stating the maxim and listing many fiqh branches based on it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'Harm must be removed', explain its definition from al-Mughrib, and identify at least five areas of fiqh that are built on this maxim.

Core Idea
Harm must be removed; this universal maxim governs rulings from sales to criminal penalties.

The first major legal maxim in fiqh, 'Harm must be removed', is a foundational principle that permeates all chapters of Islamic law. This lesson unpacks its definition and surveys the many branches that rest upon it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the definition from al-Mughrib: the maxim prohibits causing harm to another initially or as retaliation.
This includes both active harm and compensatory harm.
2
Recognize the scope
Note that Hanafi scholars mention this maxim in chapters like ghash, shuf'ah, and others, indicating its wide application.
3
Categorize the branches
List the numerous branches mentioned: return due to defect, all types of options, interdiction, pre-emption, retribution, fixed punishments, expiations, liability, compulsion for partition, appointment of imams/judges, repelling aggressors, fighting polytheists and rebels.
Group them into sales/contracts, criminal, compensatory, and public order.
4
See how each branch reflects harm removal
For each branch, identify the harm being removed. For example, return due to defect removes the buyer's economic harm; pre-emption removes the harm of a forced partition or a bad neighbour.
This connects the abstract maxim to concrete rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسره في المغرب بأنه لا يضر الرجل أخاه ابتداء ولا جزاء. (انتهى). وذكره أصحابنا رحمهم الله في كتاب الغصب والشفعة وغيرهما. ويبتني على هذه القاعدة كثير من أبواب الفقه . فمن ذلك؛ الردّ بالعيب وجميع أنواع الخيارات والحجر بسائر أنواعه على المفتى به والشفعة فإنها للشريك لدفع ضرر القسمة وللجار لدفع ضرر الجار السوء (بجيرانها تغلو الديار وترخص). والقصاص والحدود والكفَّارات وضمان المتلفات والجبر على القسمة بشرطه ونصب الأئمة والقضاة ودفع الصائل وقتال المشركين والبغاة. وفي البزازية من کتاب الكراهية باع أغصان فرصاد والمشتري إذا ارتقى لقطعها يطلع على عورات الجيران؛ يؤمر بأن يخبرهم وقت الارتقاء ليستتروا مرة أو مرتين، فإن فعل وإلا رفع إلى الحاكم ليمنعه من الارتقاء. (انتهى).
And he interpreted it in al-Mughrib: that a man should not harm his brother initially or compensatorily. (End). And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned it in the book of Ghash and Shuf'ah and others. And upon this rule many chapters of fiqh are based. From that: return due to defect and all types of options, and interdiction in all its types according to the fatwa, and pre-emption because it is for the partner to repel the harm of division and for the neighbour to repel the harm of the bad neighbour (with neighbours properties become expensive or cheap), and retribution, and fixed penalties, and expiations, and guarantee for destroyed property, and compulsion for partition with its condition, and appointment of imams and judges, and repelling an aggressor, and fighting polytheists and rebels. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the book of Disliked Acts: if one sells the branches of a mulberry tree and the buyer climbs to cut them, he may see the private parts of the neighbours; he is ordered to inform them at the time of climbing to cover themselves once or twice; if he does so, and if not, then the matter is referred to the judge to prevent him from climbing. (End).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the first major legal maxim: 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed). It is defined by the author of al-Mughrib as a prohibition against causing harm to another, whether initially or as retaliation. The Hanafi scholars (our companions) mention this maxim in the chapters of Ghash (usurpation), Shuf'ah (pre-emption), and other areas. Many branches of fiqh are built upon this maxim. The author then enumerates examples: the right to return a defective item (khiyar al-ayb), all types of options (khiyarat), interdiction (hajr) in its various forms according to the relied-upon position, pre-emption for the partner to avoid the harm of forced division and for the neighbour to avoid a bad neighbour, retribution (qisas), fixed punishments (hudud), expiations (kaffarat), liability for destroyed property, compulsion for partition under conditions, appointment of imams and judges, repelling aggressors, and fighting polytheists and rebels. A specific application from al-Bazzaziyyah: if someone sells mulberry branches and the buyer climbs to cut them, he may see the neighbours' private parts; he must warn them to cover themselves once or twice; if he does not, the judge prevents him from climbing. This shows that even a lawful act (cutting one's own branches) must be regulated to prevent harm to others.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-darar): Harming another person, whether initially or as retaliation
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption to purchase a share or adjacent property to prevent harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT listed as a branch based on the maxim 'Harm must be removed'?
A) Return due to defect
B) Pre-emption for neighbour
C) Expiation for breaking an oath
D) Mandatory covering of private parts during prayer

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Harm must be removed' is a universal principle underlying many rulings, but it is restricted by the requirement that harm cannot be removed by causing another harm.

Pre-emption and the Mulberry Branch Case: Specific Applications of Harm Removal
Stating the maxim and listing many fiqh branches based on it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for pre-emption for both partners and neighbours, and apply the harm removal principle to regulate lawful acts that may cause harm to others.

Core Idea
Pre-emption protects partners and neighbours from harm; even lawful activities must be regulated to prevent harm.

The maxim 'Harm must be removed' finds concrete expression in the right of pre-emption and in rulings that regulate otherwise permissible acts. This lesson examines these specific applications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rationale for shuf'ah for partners
Pre-emption for a partner exists to remove the harm that arises from forced partition of jointly owned property. The partner can buy the share sold to an outsider to avoid having to divide the property.
This is a clear application of removing potential harm.
2
Understand shuf'ah for neighbours
Pre-emption for a neighbour exists to prevent the harm of a bad neighbour. If a neighbour sells to an undesirable person, the adjacent neighbour can pre-empt to protect their quality of life.
Hanafi school includes neighbours, unlike Shafi'i.
3
Analyse the mulberry branch case
Selling mulberry branches is a lawful transaction, but climbing to cut them may cause harm by exposing neighbours' private parts. The harm removal principle requires the buyer to warn neighbours to cover themselves once or twice; failure leads to judicial intervention.
This shows that even a lawful act can be restricted to prevent harm.
4
Note the restriction
The solution does not involve causing harm to the buyer (e.g., forbidding the sale) but requires a minimal measure (warning). If ignored, the judge stops the climbing—a proportionate response.
This reflects the sub-maxim that harm cannot be removed by causing greater harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(انتهى). وذكره أصحابنا رحمهم الله في كتاب الغصب والشفعة وغيرهما. ويبتني على هذه القاعدة كثير من أبواب الفقه . فمن ذلك؛ الردّ بالعيب وجميع أنواع الخيارات والحجر بسائر أنواعه على المفتى به والشفعة فإنها للشريك لدفع ضرر القسمة وللجار لدفع ضرر الجار السوء (بجيرانها تغلو الديار وترخص). والقصاص والحدود والكفَّارات وضمان المتلفات والجبر على القسمة بشرطه ونصب الأئمة والقضاة ودفع الصائل وقتال المشركين والبغاة. وفي البزازية من کتاب الكراهية باع أغصان فرصاد والمشتري إذا ارتقى لقطعها يطلع على عورات الجيران؛ يؤمر بأن يخبرهم وقت الارتقاء ليستتروا مرة أو مرتين، فإن فعل وإلا رفع إلى الحاكم ليمنعه من الارتقاء. What is the ruling in the mulberry branch case if the buyer climbs without warning the neighbours?

Key Takeaway

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

The Maxim of Necessity and Its Hanafi Restrictions
Stating the second related maxim and expanding with Shafi'i addition and Hanafi responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Necessities permit prohibitions' by identifying its scope and the Hanafi restrictions based on weighing harms, and evaluate the Shafi'i condition of non-deficiency.

Core Idea
Necessity permits the prohibited, but only to the extent of the lesser harm.

This lesson examines the second major maxim of Islamic jurisprudence: 'Necessities permit prohibitions.' While the maxim appears to grant broad permission, the Hanafi school, through detailed case law, imposes critical restrictions based on comparing harms. We will study the examples from the text, the Shafi'i addition regarding the prophet's corpse, and the Hanafi responses that establish a nuanced application of this maxim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its apparent scope
Read the opening statement: 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات.' Recognize that this is a major legal maxim (qa'ida kubra) that applies across ibadat, muamalat, and jinayat. Note the immediate examples given: eating carrion, drinking wine to swallow food, uttering disbelief under duress, taking property of a debtor, and repelling an aggressor.
The examples illustrate situations where a normally prohibited act becomes permissible due to extreme necessity.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i condition and its implication
The Shafi'is add a condition: the prohibited thing must not be diminished in sanctity. Their example is a dead prophet – it cannot be eaten even by the starving because the prophet's sanctity exceeds the value of the starving person's life. Understand that this condition restricts the maxim by introducing a hierarchy of values.
This condition is a form of 'deficiency' in the object of the prohibition.
3
Examine the Hanafi responses as analogous restrictions
The Hanafi scholars respond with two rulings: (1) someone compelled to kill another under threat of being killed must not do so, because his own death is a lesser harm; (2) a body buried without shroud or washing is not exhumed because violating the grave's sanctity is worse than the deficiency in rites. These rulings show that the Hanafi school also restricts the maxim, but through a harm-weighted analysis rather than a condition on the object.
These responses effectively achieve the same result as the Shafi'i condition but through a different methodology: weighing harms (tawazun al-mafasid).
4
Synthesize the Hanafi approach to the maxim
Conclude that the Hanafi position applies the maxim with an inbuilt limitation: the permissible act must be the lesser of two evils. This is expressed through sub-maxims like 'Harm may not be removed by a greater harm' (الضرر لا يزال بالضرر) and 'Choose the lesser of two evils' (أهون الشرين). The maxim is therefore not absolute.
This synthesis prepares you for applying the maxim in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : الضرورات تبيح المحظورات، ومن ثم جاز أكل الميتة عند المخمصة، وإساغة اللقمة بالخمر، والتلفّظ بكلمة الكفر للإكراه، وكذا إتلاف المال وأخذ مال الممتنع من أداء الدين بغير إذنه ودفع الصائل، ولو أدى إلى قتله. وزاد الشافعية على هذه القاعدة بشرط عدم نقصانها ؛ قالوا ليخرج ما لو كان الميت نبيًّا، فإنه لا يحلّ أكله للمضطر، لأن حرمته أعظم في نظر الشرع من مهجة المضطر . (انتهى). ولكن ذكر أصحابنا رحمهم الله ما يفيده؛ فإنهم قالوا: لو أكره على قتل غيره بقتل لا يرخص له، فإن قتله أيْمَ لأن مفسدة قتل نفسه أخف من مفسدة قتل غيره. وقالوا لو دفن بلا تكفين لا ينبش منه؛ لأن مفسدة هتك أشد حرمته من عدم تكفينه الذي قام الستر بالتراب مقامه. وكذا قالوا: لو دفن بلا غسل وأهيل عليه التراب؛ صلي على قبره ولا يخرج.
The first: Necessities permit prohibitions. Hence it is permissible to eat carrion when one is starving, to swallow a morsel with wine, to utter a word of disbelief under duress, and likewise destroying property and taking the property of one who refuses to pay a debt without his permission, and repelling an aggressor even if it leads to killing him. The Shafi'is added to this rule a condition: that the forbidden thing does not become deficient; they said to exclude the case where the dead body is a prophet, for it is not permissible for the starving person to eat it, because his sanctity in the view of the Law is greater than the life of the starving person. (End). But our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned something that conveys its meaning; for they said: if one is compelled to kill another by being killed himself, it is not permitted for him (i.e., he may not kill the other), because the harm of killing himself is lighter than the harm of killing another. And they said: if one is buried without a shroud, he is not to be exhumed, because the harm of violating his sanctity is greater than the lack of shrouding, for which the covering by earth has taken its place. Similarly they said: if one is buried without washing and earth is thrown over him, the funeral prayer is performed over his grave and he is not taken out.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the second related maxim: 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات' (Necessities permit prohibitions). It gives examples: eating carrion when starving, swallowing a morsel with wine (if one is choking and water is unavailable), uttering a word of disbelief under duress, destroying property or taking the property of someone who refuses to pay a debt, and repelling an aggressor even if it kills him. The Shafi'is add a condition that the prohibited thing must not be diminished in sanctity; e.g., if the dead body is a prophet, it may not be eaten even by the starving, because the prophet's sanctity outweighs the starving person's life. The Hanafi scholars respond with two related rulings: (1) If someone is compelled to kill another (by threat of being killed himself), he may not kill the other, because the harm of killing himself (by refusing) is lighter than the harm of killing an innocent person. (2) If someone is buried without a shroud or without washing, the grave is not to be disturbed because the harm of violating the corpse's sanctity outweighs the deficiency in the funeral rites; the earth serves as a covering. This shows that the Hanafi school also recognizes a hierarchy of harms, similar to the Shafi'i condition of 'no deficiency' but expressed through specific rulings.

Key Terms
الضرورات (al-darurat): Extreme necessity that threatens life or essential wellbeing
المخمصة (al-makhmasah): Extreme hunger that risks death
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion or duress that overrides free choice

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars, if a person is compelled to kill another under threat of being killed himself, what is the ruling?
A) He may kill the other to save his own life
B) He must not kill the other, because the harm of killing himself is lighter
C) He may kill the other only if the other is a combatant
D) He must kill the other and then seek repentance

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Necessities permit prohibitions' is always qualified by the restriction that the permitted act must be the lesser harm; the Hanafi school achieves this through harm weighing rather than a condition on the object.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Harm Cannot Be Removed by Harm and the Principle of Bearing Private Harm for Public Good
Stating the third major maxim and its refinement regarding bearing private harm for public good

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-darar la yuzal bi al-darar' and its refinement 'yatuhammal al-darar al-khas li ajl daf' al-darar al-'am' to identify when private harm is tolerated for public good, and to analyze related furuat.

Core Idea
You cannot remove a harm by causing another harm, but a private harm may be tolerated when necessary to prevent a greater public harm.

The third major maxim of Hanafi fiqh, 'Harm cannot be removed by harm,' restricts the first maxim, 'Harm must be removed.' This lesson unpacks the maxim through concrete branches from partnership, family law, and extreme necessity. It also introduces a crucial refinement: when a private harm serves to repel a greater public harm, it is tolerated. Understanding this balance is essential for a mufti weighing conflicting interests.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its restriction
Recognize that 'al-darar la yuzal bi al-darar' is a restriction on 'al-darar yuzal'. The first maxim commands removing harm, but the third maxim prohibits using another harm as the means.
The maxim is from the major qawaid kubra.
2
Examine the first branch: co-owner building
A co-owner is not compelled to build on common property because forcing him would cause him financial harm. The builder may detain the property to recover costs, but the other owner is not harmed beyond the detention.
The ruling distinguishes between building without judge's permission (recovers value) and with permission (recovers expenses).
3
Examine the second branch: master not compelled to marry slave
A master is not compelled to marry off his slave or slave-girl, even if they suffer harm from being unmarried. Forcing the master would harm his authority and finances. The slave's desire does not override the master's right to avoid harm.
This shows the maxim protects the master from harm caused by the slave's need.
4
Examine the third branch: starving persons
Two starving persons: one has food, the other does not. The hungry one may not take the food or harm the other's body. The one with food is not compelled to share because that would harm him. This is an extreme case affirming the maxim.
This branch shows the maxim's strict application even in necessity.
5
Understand the tanbih: private vs public harm
The note 'yatuhammal al-darar al-khas li ajl daf' al-darar al-'am' qualifies the maxim. When a private harm is necessary to prevent a greater public harm, it is tolerated. This refines the earlier maxim 'al-darar yuzal bi mithlihi'
This is a dabt (restriction) that allows exceptions to protect public interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : الضرر لا يزال بالضرر، وهي مقيدة لقولهم : الضرر يزال، أي لا بضرر. ومن فروعها عدم وجوب العمارة على الشريك، وإنما يُقال لمُريدها أنفق واحبس العين إلى استيفاء قيمة البناء أو ما أنفقته فالأول إن كان بغير إذن القاضي، والثاني إن كان بإذنه وهو المعتمد . وكتبنا في شرح الكنز في مسائل شتى من كتاب القضاء أن الشريك يجبر عليها في ثلاث مسائل، ولا يجبر السيد على تزويج عبده أو أمته وإن تضرر، أو لا يأكل المضطر طعام مضطر آخر ولا شيئًا من بدنه . تنبيه : يتحمل الضرر الخاص لأجل دفع الضرر العام. وهذا مقيد لقولهم: الضرر يزال بمثله. وعليه فروع كثيرة.
The third: Harm cannot be removed by harm, and it is a restriction to their statement: Harm must be removed, i.e., not by harm. Among its branches: The share (partner) is not obligated to build (upon the common property). Rather it is said to the one who wants to build: Spend and retain the property until you recover the value of the construction or what you spent. The first (recovering value) is if it was without the judge's permission, and the second (recovering expenses) is if it was with his permission, and that is the relied-upon view. And we wrote in the commentary of al-Kanz in various issues from the book of Judgments that the partner is compelled to build in three issues. And the master is not compelled to marry off his slave or slave-girl, even if they suffer harm. And the starving person may not eat the food of another starving person, nor anything from his body. Note: Private harm is tolerated to repel general harm. This is a restriction to their statement: Harm is removed by its like. And upon this there are many branches.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the third major maxim: 'الضرر لا يزال بالضرر' (Harm cannot be removed by harm). This is a restriction on the first maxim 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed). The meaning is that while harm must be eliminated, one cannot eliminate harm by causing another harm. Several branches illustrate this: (1) In a shared property, a co-owner (sharik) is not compelled to contribute to construction; if one co-owner wants to build, he may do so at his own expense and then detain the property (or a share) until he recovers the cost. If he builds without the judge's permission, he recovers the value added; if with permission, he recovers his actual expenses. The latter is the relied-upon position. However, the author notes in his commentary on al-Kanz that there are three exceptions where a co-owner is compelled to build (these are from the book of Judgments). (2) The owner of a slave or slave-girl (member of his household) is not compelled to marry them off, even if they suffer harm from being unmarried; the master's authority is respected, and forcing him would cause him harm. (3) A starving person may not eat the food of another starving person, nor any part of his body, because that would harm the other person. The passage ends with an important note (tanbih): 'يتحمل الضرر الخاص لأجل دفع الضرر العام' (Private harm is tolerated to repel general harm). This is a further restriction on the maxim 'الضرر يزال بمثله' (harm is removed by its like), meaning that when a private harm is necessary to prevent a greater public harm, the private harm is borne. The author says there are many branches based on this principle.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Not used in this passage; but the maxim restricts harmful application of pre-emption
الضرر الخاص (al-darar al-khas): Harm affecting an individual or a limited group, as opposed to general public harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'Harm cannot be removed by harm,' which of the following is correct regarding a starving person (mudtarr) who has no food but another mudtarr has food?
A) The hungry one may take the food by force because his life is in danger.
B) The one with food must share it under the rule of necessity.
C) The hungry one may not take the food, and the owner is not compelled to share.
D) Both may share equally, as necessity overrides the maxim.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Harm cannot be removed by harm' restricts how harm is eliminated, but it can be overridden when a private harm is necessary to prevent a greater public harm.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
12 min
Harm is Not Removed by Harm: The Third Major Maxim and Its Applications
Stating the maxim and listing extensive applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'Harm is not removed by harm', identify its two restrictions (private harm borne for public good and choosing the lesser harm), and apply it to at least five furu' from the text.

Core Idea
Harm is not removed by harm; but a greater harm may be removed by a lesser one.

The maxim 'Harm must be removed' is universal, but it does not allow causing one harm to remove another. This lesson examines the third major maxim, which qualifies that principle. We will work through the text's extensive applications to understand how the Hanafi school balances competing harms.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its relationship
The maxim 'al-darar la yuzal bi al-darar' restricts 'al-darar yuzal': harm is removed, but not by inflicting another harm.
This is a qayd (restriction) on the earlier maxim.
2
Identify the first restriction: private harm for public good
The text's first tanbih: 'yutahammal al-darar al-khas li ajl daf' al-darar al-amm' – private harm may be borne to prevent public harm.
Examples: shooting at enemies with child shields, demolishing leaning wall, restricting harmful professionals.
3
Identify the second restriction: choosing the lesser harm
The second tanbih: when one harm is greater, the severe is removed by the lighter (al-ashadd yuzal bi al-akhaff).
Examples: imprisoning father for child support, usurper's building rules.
4
Apply the maxim to the furu'
Work through the listed branches, identifying which restriction applies and how the harm is balanced.
Use the template: [CASE] → [Which harm?] → [Private/public?] → [Greater/lesser?] → [Ruling].
5
Note the authority and mufta bihi
The maxim is from Ibn Nujaym's Ashbah, part of fatawa mutakhkhirin. It is universally accepted; its restrictions are operative in fatwa.
No ikhtilaf on the maxim itself, only on specific applications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : الضرر لا يزال بالضرر، وهي مقيدة لقولهم : الضرر يزال، أي لا بضرر. ومن فروعها عدم وجوب العمارة على الشريك، وإنما يُقال لمُريدها أنفق واحبس العين إلى استيفاء قيمة البناء أو ما أنفقته فالأول إن كان بغير إذن القاضي، والثاني إن كان بإذنه وهو المعتمد . وكتبنا في شرح الكنز في مسائل شتى من كتاب القضاء أن الشريك يجبر عليها في ثلاث مسائل، ولا يجبر السيد على تزويج عبده أو أمته وإن تضرر، أو لا يأكل المضطر طعام مضطر آخر ولا شيئًا من بدنه . تنبيه يتحمل الضرر الخاص لأجل دفع الضرر العام. وهذا مقيد لقولهم: الضرر يزال بمثله. وعليه فروع كثيرة. منها : جواز الرمي إلى كفَّار تترسوا بصبيان المسلمين. ومنها: وجوب نقض حائط مملوك مال إلى طريق العامَّة على مالكها، دفعا للضرر العام . ومنها: جواز الحجز على البالغ العاقل الحرّ عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ثلاث : المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري ،المفلس، دفعا للضرر العام . ومنها: جوازه على السفيه عندهما وعليه الفتوى، دفعا للضرر العام . ومنها : بيع وهو المعتمد . مال المديون المحبوس عندهما لقضاء دينه، دفعًا للضرر عن الغرماء . ومنها : التسعير عند تعدّي أرباب الطعام في بيعه بغبن فاحش. ومنها : بيع طعام المحتكر جبرًا عليه عند الحاجة وامتناعه من البيع، دفعا للضرر العام . ومنها منع اتخاذ حانوت للطبخ بين البزازين وكذا كل ضرر عام، كذا في الكافي وغيره وتمامه في شرح منظومة ابن وهبان من الدعوى. تنبیه آخر تقييد القاعدة أيضًا بما لو كان أحدهما أعظم ضررًا من الآخر؛ فإن الأشدّ يُزال بالأخفّ، فمن ذلك الإجبار على قضاء الدين، والنفقات الواجبات. ومنها : حبس الأب لو امتنع عن الإنفاق على ولده بخلاف الدين. ومنها : لو غصب ،ساجة أي خشبة وأدخلها في بنائه ؛ فإن كانت قيمة البناء أكثر يملكها صاحبه بالقيمة وإن كانت قيمتها أكثر من قيمته لم ينقطع حق المالك عنها . ومنها : لو غصب أرضًا فبنى فيها أو غرس ؛ فإن كانت قيمة الأرض أكثر قلعا وردت وإلا ضمن له قيمتها . ومنها : لو ابتلعت دجاجة لؤلؤة؛ ينظر إلى أكثرهما قيمة فيضمن صاحب الأكثر قيمة الأقل. وعلى هذا لو أدخل فصيل غيره في داره فكبَّر فيها ولم يمكن إخراجه إلا بهدم الجدار، وكذا لو أدخل البقر رأسه في قدر من النحاس فتعذر إخراجه، هكذا الله ، كما ذكره الزيلعي في كتاب الغصب. ذكر أصحابنا رحمهم الله
Third: Harm is not removed by harm, and it restricts their statement: Harm is removed, i.e., not with harm. And among its branches: There is no obligation of construction upon a partner, but the one who wants it is told: Spend and retain the property until recovery of the value of the building or what you spent; the first if without judge's permission, the second if with his permission, and that is the relied-upon. And we wrote in Sharh al-Kanz in various issues from the book of qada that the partner is forced upon it in three issues, and the master is not forced to marry his slave or slave-girl even if harmed, and the one in dire need does not eat the food of another in dire need nor anything from his body. Caution: Private harm is borne to repel public harm. This restricts their statement: Harm is removed by its like. And upon it are many branches: Among them: Permissibility of shooting at disbelievers who shield themselves with Muslim children. Among them: Obligation of demolishing a wall owned by someone that leans towards the public road, to repel public harm. Among them: Permissibility of placing a (legal) restriction on a sane adult free person according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) three: the immoral mufti, the ignorant physician, and the insolvent carrier; to repel public harm. Among them: Permissibility upon the foolish person according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the fatwa is upon it, to repel public harm. Among them: Sale (and it is the relied-upon) of the property of the imprisoned debtor according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) to repay his debt, to repel harm from creditors. Among them: Price control when food traders exceed in selling with gross fraud. Among them: Forced sale of the hoarder's food when there is need and he refuses to sell, to repel public harm. Among them: Preventing the establishment of a cooking shop among cloth merchants, and likewise every public harm, as in al-Kafi and others, and its completion is in Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban from the chapter of claims. Another caution: The maxim is also restricted to cases where one of the two harms is greater than the other; then the severe is removed by the lighter. Among that: Compulsion to repay debt and obligatory maintenance. Among them: Imprisonment of the father if he refuses to spend on his child, unlike debt. Among them: If he usurped a beam (i.e., a piece of wood) and inserted it into his building; then if the value of the building is more, the owner takes it by paying its value, but if its value is more than the building's value, the owner's right is not cut off from it. Among them: If he usurped land and built or planted on it; then if the land's value is more, he removes and returns it, otherwise he is liable for its value. Among them: If a chicken swallowed a pearl; one looks at which is more valuable, and the owner of the more valuable guarantees the value of the less. Similarly, if someone brought someone else's young animal into his house and it grew large and cannot be taken out except by demolishing the wall, and likewise if an ox inserted its head into a copper pot and could not remove it, as mentioned by al-Zayla'i in the book of Ghasb. Our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the third major Hanafi maxim: 'Harm is not removed by harm.' It qualifies the earlier maxim 'Harm must be removed' by specifying that you cannot remove one harm by causing another. The text lists branches: a partner is not forced to contribute to construction except in three cases; a master cannot be forced to marry his slave; a person in dire need cannot eat another's food or body. Then a tanbih (caution) introduces a qualification: private harm may be endured to prevent greater public harm. This is followed by many applications: shooting at enemies using Muslim child shields, demolishing a dangerous wall, restricting immoral mufti, ignorant doctor, insolvent carrier, and others, forced sale of debtor's property, price controls, forced sale of hoarded food, preventing nuisance businesses. Another tanbih adds that when one harm is greater, the lesser harm is inflicted to remove the greater. Examples include compelling debt repayment, father's imprisonment for child support, and rules for usurped property and animals.

Key Terms
ضرر (darar): Harm or injury that is legally recognized

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on shooting at disbelievers who shield themselves with Muslim children?
A) Absolutely forbidden
B) Permissible to repel public harm
C) Permissible only if children are already dead
D) Requires fatwa from a mujtahid

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Harm is not removed by harm' restricts the removal of harm to permissible means, balancing public and private interests and preferring the lesser harm when necessary.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Applications of 'Harm Must Be Removed' and the Fourth Derived Rule
Listing furuat of al-darar yuzal and deriving a fourth rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-darar yuzal' to new cases by identifying the greater harm and choosing the lesser, and to construct the fourth derived rule 'irtikab akhaff al-dararayn'.

Core Idea
When two harms are unavoidable, the greater harm is removed by committing the lesser.

This passage presents a rich collection of cases where harm must be removed, even at the cost of some individual loss. It then crystallizes a fourth derived rule: when two harmful matters conflict, the greater is avoided by the lesser. Understanding these applications prepares the mufti to weigh harms in real-world fatwa derivation.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the qawida 'al-darar yuzal' and note that it applies to both public harm (darar 'amm) and private harm. The scope covers all fiqh chapters, especially mu'amalat and jinayat.
The maxim is one of the five major qawaid kubra.
2
Analyze the furuat
Review each listed case (shooting at shields, demolishing wall, legal restriction on professionals, etc.) and verify how the maxim is applied: which harm is being removed and which lesser harm is permitted.
Pay attention to whether harm is public or private.
3
Identify the fourth derived rule
Extract the rule 'irtikab akhaff al-dararayn' (committing the lesser of two harms) from the passage's last section. Understand that when two harmful matters (musfidatan) conflict, the greater harm is avoided by choosing the lighter.
Al-Zayla'i's principle: if two trials are equal, any choice; if different, choose the lighter.
4
Examine the Shafi'i ikhtilaf
Compare the Hanafi position on the cow-and-pot case (weigh values) with the Shafi'i differentiation (based on owner presence and edibility). Note the thamarah al-khilaf in compensation and breaking the pot.
The Hanafi position is simpler and based on the maxim directly.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Practice applying the derived rule to a modern case, such as a landlord wanting to evict a tenant with no alternative housing. Identify the greater harm and state the ruling using the maxim.
Always ensure the harms are undeniable and unavoidable.

منها : جواز الرمي إلى كفَّار تترسوا بصبيان المسلمين.
ومنها: وجوب نقض حائط مملوك مال إلى طريق العامَّة على مالكها، دفعا للضرر العام .
ومنها: جواز الحجز على البالغ العاقل الحرّ عند أبي حنيفة رحمه ثلاث : المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري ،المفلس، دفعا للضرر العام .
ومنها: جوازه على السفيه عندهما وعليه الفتوى، دفعا للضرر العام .
ومنها : بيع مال المديون المحبوس عندهما لقضاء دينه، دفعًا للضرر عن الغرماء وهو المعتمد .
ومنها : التسعير عند تعدّي أرباب الطعام في بيعه بغبن فاحش.
ومنها : بيع طعام المحتكر جبرًا عليه عند الحاجة وامتناعه من البيع، دفعا للضرر العام .
ومنها منع اتخاذ حانوت للطبخ بين البزازين وكذا كل ضرر عام، كذا في الكافي وغيره وتمامه في شرح منظومة ابن وهبان من الدعوى.
تنبیه آخر
تقييد القاعدة أيضًا بما لو كان أحدهما أعظم ضررًا من الآخر؛ فإن الأشدّ يُزال بالأخفّ، فمن ذلك الإجبار على قضاء الدين، والنفقات الواجبات.
ومنها : حبس الأب لو امتنع عن الإنفاق على ولده بخلاف الدين. ومنها : لو غصب ساجة أي خشبة وأدخلها في بنائه ؛ فإن كانت قيمة البناء أكثر يملكها صاحبه بالقيمة وإن كانت قيمتها أكثر من قيمته لم ينقطع حق المالك عنها .
ومنها : لو غصب أرضًا فبنى فيها أو غرس ؛ فإن كانت قيمة الأرض أكثر قلعا وردت وإلا ضمن له قيمتها .
ومنها : لو ابتلعت دجاجة لؤلؤة؛ ينظر إلى أكثرهما قيمة فيضمن صاحب الأكثر قيمة الأقل. وعلى هذا لو أدخل فصيل غيره في داره فكبَّر فيها ولم يمكن إخراجه إلا بهدم الجدار، وكذا لو أدخل البقر رأسه في قدر من النحاس فتعذر إخراجه، هكذا ذكر أصحابنا رحمهم الله، كما ذكره الزيلعي في كتاب الغصب.
وفصل الشافعية؛ فقالوا : إن كان صاحب البهيمة معها فهو مفرط بترك الحفظ فإن كانت غير مأكولة كسرت القدر وعليه أرش النقص، أو مأكولة ففي ذبحها وجهان. وإن لم يكن معها فإن فرط صاحب القدر كسرت ولا أرش، وإلا فله الأرش.
وينبغي أن يلحق بمسألة البقرة ما لو سقط ديناره في محبرة غيره ولم يخرج إلا بكسرها.
ومنها : جواز دخول بيت غيره إذا سقط متاعه فيه وخاف صاحبه أنه لو طلبه منه لأخفاه .
ومنها : مسألة الظفر بجنس دينه .
ومنها جواز شق بطن الميتة لإخراج الولد إذا كانت تُرجى حياته. وقد أمر أبو حنيفة رحمه الله فعاش الولد كما في الملتقط قالوا بخلاف ما إذا ابتلع لؤلؤة فمات فإنه لا يشق بطنه لأن حرمة الآدمي أعظم من حرمة المال. وسوى الشافعية بينهما في جواز الشق. وفي تهذيب القلانسي من الحظر والإباحة وقيمة الدرة في تَرِكته وإن لم يترك شيئًا لا يجب شيء. (انتهى).
ومنها طلب صاحب الأكثر القسمة وشريكه يتضرر ؛ فإن صاحب الكثير يُجاب على أحد الأقوال لأن ضرره في عدم القسمة أعظم من ضرر شريكه بها.
الرابعة :
نشأت من هذه القاعدة قاعدة رابعة، وهي ما إذا تعارض مسفدتان رُوعِيَ أعظمهما ضررًا بارتكاب أخفّهما. قال الزيلعي في باب شروط الصلاة ثم الأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أن من ابتلي ببليتين وهما متساويتان يأخذ بأيتهما شاء، وإن اختلفا يختار أهونهما لأن مباشرة الحرام لا تجوز إلا للضرورة، ولا ضرورة في حق الزيادة .
مثاله : رجل عليه جرح لو سجد سال جرحه وإن لم يسجد لم يسل، فإنه يصلّي يوميء بالركوع والسجود لأن ترك السجود أهون من الصلاة مع الحدث. ألا ترى أن ترك السجود جائز حالة الاختيار في التطوع على الدابة. ومع الحدث لا يجوز بحال. وكذا شيخ لا يقدر على القراءة قائمًا ويقدر عليها قاعدا، يصلي قاعدا لأنه يجوز حالة الاختيار في النفل ولا يجوز ترك القراءة بحال، ولو صلى في الفصلين مع الحدث وترك القراءة لم يجز. ولو كان معه ثوبان نجاسة كل واحد منهما ...
Among them: Permissibility of shooting at disbelievers who shield themselves with Muslim children. And among them: Obligation of demolishing a owned wall that leans into the public road, at the owner's expense, to remove public harm. And among them: Permissibility of placing legal restriction on an adult sane free person according to Abu Hanifa – three: the dissolute mufti, the ignorant physician, and the bankrupt transporter – to remove public harm. And among them: Permissibility of placing restriction on a wasteful person according to the two companions, and the fatwa is upon it, to remove public harm. And among them: Selling the wealth of an imprisoned debtor according to the two companions to pay his debt, to remove harm from creditors, and this is the relied-upon view. And among them: Price-fixing when food sellers transgress by selling with excessive fraud. And among them: Forcible sale of the hoarder's food when needed and he refuses to sell, to remove public harm. And among them: Preventing the establishment of a cooking shop among cloth merchants, and likewise every public harm – as in al-Kafi and others, and its completion in the commentary on Ibn Wahban's poem from the chapter of claims. Another note: Restricting the rule also to when one harm is greater than the other; for the severe is removed by the lighter. Among that: Compelling payment of debt and obligatory maintenance. And among them: Imprisonment of the father if he refuses to spend on his child, unlike (non-maintenance) debt. And among them: If someone usurps a beam or wood and inserts it into his building; if the value of the building is more, its owner takes it by paying its value; but if the value of the beam is more than the building, the right of the owner is not cut off. And among them: If one usurps land and builds or plants; if the land's value is more, he tears down and returns; otherwise, its value is guaranteed to him. And among them: If a chicken swallows a pearl; one looks at which of the two is greater in value, and the owner of the more valuable guarantees the value of the lesser. And upon this, if one brings another's calf into his house and it grows big and cannot be removed except by demolishing the wall; similarly, if a cow puts its head into a copper pot and cannot be removed – thus our companions mentioned, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in the book of usurpation. The Shafi'is differentiated: they said if the owner of the animal is present, he is negligent by failing to guard; if the animal is not edible, the pot is broken and he pays for the damage; if edible, there are two opinions on slaughtering. If he is not present, then if the pot's owner was negligent, it is broken with no compensation; otherwise, he gets compensation. And it is appropriate to attach to the cow case the case of a dinar falling into another's inkwell and not coming out except by breaking it. And among them: Permissibility of entering another's house if his property has fallen in and he fears that if he asks for it, the owner would hide it. And among them: The issue of seizing one's own like debt. And among them: Permissibility of slitting the belly of a deceased woman to extract a child if its life is hoped for. Abu Hanifa ordered it and the child lived, as in al-Multaqat. They said unlike if one swallowed a pearl and died, then his belly is not slit because the sanctity of a human is greater than the sanctity of wealth. The Shafi'is equated the two cases in permissibility of slitting. In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi from the section of prohibition and permissibility: and the value of the pearl is in his estate; if he leaves nothing, nothing is due. (End.) And among them: The request of the majority owner for partition while his partner is harmed; the majority owner is answered according to one opinion because his harm in not partitioning is greater than the partner's harm by it. The fourth: A fourth rule derived from this rule, which is when two harmful matters conflict, the greater of them is observed by committing the lighter. Al-Zayla'i said in the chapter of conditions of prayer: The principle in this type of issue is that one who is tried with two trials, if they are equal, he takes whichever he wishes; if they differ, he chooses the lighter of them because committing the forbidden is not permitted except for necessity, and there is no necessity regarding the extra part. Example: A man has a wound; if he prostrates, his wound flows; if he does not prostrate, it does not flow. He prays by gesturing with bowing and prostration because leaving prostration is lighter than praying with impurity. Do you not see that leaving prostration is permissible in optional state in supererogatory prayer on an animal? While with impurity it is not permissible at all. Likewise, an old man who cannot recite standing but can do so sitting, prays sitting because it is permissible in optional state in supererogatory prayer, and leaving recitation is not permissible at all. If he prays in both cases with impurity and leaving recitation, it is not valid. And if he has two garments, each with impurity...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a collection of applications (furuat) of the well-known maxim 'الضرر يزال' (harm must be removed), followed by a derived fourth rule 'ارتكاب أخف الضررين' (committing the lesser harm). The author begins by listing various cases where public or private harm is removed, even if it causes some individual damage: shooting at enemy shields, demolishing a dangerous wall, restriction on certain professionals, sale of debtor's property, price control, breaking a hoarder's monopoly, and preventing nuisance trades. Then a 'تنبيه آخر' (another note) clarifies that the maxim is applied only when one harm is greater; the severer is removed by the lighter. Examples follow: compelling maintenance, imprisonment for non-maintenance, and property usurpation cases where the greater value determines the outcome. The next paragraph records a disagreement with the Shafi'is regarding a cow stuck in a pot: the Shafi'is differentiate based on owner presence and edibility, while the Hanafis (implied) consider the greater harm without such details. Further applications include entering a house for lost property, seizure of one's own debt, slitting a deceased woman's belly to save a child (contrasted with a pearl-swallower to highlight human sanctity over wealth), and partition disputes where the majority owner's greater harm is preferred. Finally, the 'الرابعة' (fourth) rule is explicitly stated: when two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser. Al-Zayla'i's principle from Kitab al-Salat is quoted: if two trials are equal, any choice; if different, choose the lighter – illustrated with a wounded man praying by gesture rather than with impurity, and an old man sitting to recite rather than omitting recitation. The passage ends mid-sentence about two impure garments. Throughout, the author maintains a Hanafi perspective, presenting positions of Abu Hanifa, the two companions, and occasional Shafi'i views for contrast. The authority weight of these rulings is from the later works (al-Kafi, al-Zayla'i) and not necessarily from Zahir al-Riwayah, but they are accepted as mufta bihi in the madhhab.

Key Terms
الضرر يزال (al-darar yuzal): The legal maxim that harm must be removed, even if it requires allowing a lesser harm or violating a minor prohibition
ارتكاب أخف الضررين (irtikab akhaff al-dararayn): Committing the lesser of two harms when they conflict, derived from the maxim 'الضرر يزال'
مسفدتان (musfidatan): Two harmful matters or corruptions that conflict, requiring choice of the lesser

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as an application of 'al-darar yuzal' in the passage?
A) Shooting at shielded enemies
B) Demolishing a dangerous wall
C) Forcing a debtor to sell his property
D) Allowing a husband to beat his wife

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar yuzal' and its derived rule 'irtikab akhaff al-dararayn' are fundamental tools for a mufti to resolve conflicts of harms in fiqh.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
12 min
Fifth Maxim: Repelling Harm Takes Precedence over Bringing Benefit
Presentation of the fifth maxim with its textual proof and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Dar' al-mafasid awla min jalb al-masalih' to practical fiqh cases and identify when an exception (benefit overcoming harm) applies.

Core Idea
Prohibitions carry more weight than commands; thus avoiding harm takes priority over securing benefit.

This lesson presents one of the five major legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra) in Hanafi fiqh: 'Repelling harms takes precedence over bringing benefits.' The maxim is grounded in the Prophetic command to avoid prohibitions entirely while limiting commands to one's capacity. We will explore its textual proof, a range of furuat (sub-cases) from purification to prayer, and the crucial exception when the benefit clearly outweighs the harm. Understanding this maxim equips the mufti to resolve conflicting interests in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the maxim: 'درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المصالح'. Recognise it as a kulliya (universal principle) governing all fiqh chapters, especially when harm and benefit conflict.
The maxim is ranked among the five kubra (major) maxims.
2
Identify the textual proof (dalil naqli)
Locate the hadith: 'When I command you, do as much as you can; when I forbid you, avoid it entirely.' Understand that this shows the Shari'ah's greater emphasis on prohibitions.
The narration from al-Kashf about abandoning a forbidden atom being better than worship reinforces the point.
3
Analyse the furuat: apply the maxim
Work through each furu' case (istinja', ghusl, mouthwash, combing, prayer conditions, lying) and see how the maxim yields the ruling. For each, identify the harm being repelled and the benefit being sacrificed.
Note the differentiation between man and woman in ghusl: woman postpones due to stronger harm (exposure), man does not because janabah is stronger.
4
Identify the istithna' (exception)
When the benefit clearly outweighs the harm, the maxim is inverted: benefit is sought despite the harm. Examples include praying when a condition is impossible, and lying for reconciliation.
This exception is governed by the related maxim: 'المصلحة تغلب المفسدة' (benefit overcomes harm).
5
Relate to other qawaid
Connect this maxim to 'يرتكب أخف الضررين' (the lesser harm is committed) and 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' (hardship brings ease). Understand how they interact in fatwa.
The maxim is a tool for tarjih (preference) when conflicting evidences or interests arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : ونظير القاعدة الرابعة قاعدة خامسة؛ وهي درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المصالح. فإذا تعارضت مفسدة ومصلحة قدَّم دفع المفسدة غالبا لأن اعتناء الشرع بالمنهيات أشد من اعتنائه بالمأمورات، ولذا قال عليه السلام: «إذا أمرتكم بشيء منه ما استطعتم وإذا نهيتكم عن شيءٍ فاجتنبوه (۱) ورُوِيَ في الكشف حديثا لترك ذرة مما نهى الله عنه أفضل من عبادة «الثقلين ومن ثم جاز ترك الواجب دفعا للمشقة، ولم يسامح في الإقدام على المنهيات خصوصا الكبائر. ومن ذلك ما ذكره البزازي في فتاواه : ومَن لم يجد سترة ترك الاستنجاء ولو على شط نهر لأن النهي راجح على الأمر حتى استوعب النهي الأزمان ولم يقتض الأمر التكرار. (انتهى). والمرأة إذا وجب عليها الغسل ولم تجد سترة من الرجال تؤخره، بخلاف الرجل إذا لم يجد سترة من الرجال لا يؤخره ويغتسل. وفي الاستنجاء إذا لم يجد سترة يتركه. والفرق أن النجاسة الحكمية أقوى والمرأة بين النساء كالرجل بين الرجال، كذا في شرح النقاية . ومن فروع ذلك المبالغة في المضمضة والاستنشاق مسنونة وتكره للصائم. وتخليل الشعر سُنّة في الطهارة ويُكره للمحرم. وقد تُراعى المصلحة لغلبتها على المفسدة؛ فمن ذلك الصلاة اختلال شرط من شروطها من الطهارة أو الستر أو مع الاستقبال فإن في كل ذلك مفسدة لما فيه من الإخلال بجلال الله تعالى في أن لا يناجي إلا على أكمل الأحوال ومتى تعذر عليه شيء من ذلك جازت الصلاة بدونه تقديمًا لمصلحة الصلاة على هذه المفسدة. ومنه الكذب مفسدة محرمة وهو متى تضمن جلب مصلحة تربو عليه جاز كالكذب للإصلاح بين الناس وعلى الزوجة لإصلاحها. وهذا النوع راجع إلى ارتكاب أخف المفسدتين في الحقيقة.
Fifth: And similar to the fourth base is a fifth base: repelling harms is more appropriate than bringing benefits. So when a harm and a benefit conflict, repelling the harm is given precedence usually, because the Shari'ah's concern with prohibitions is stronger than its concern with commands. Therefore, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When I command you with something, do of it as much as you are able; when I forbid you from something, avoid it entirely.' And it is narrated in al-Kashf a hadith: 'Abandoning an atom of what Allah has forbidden is better than the worship of the two weighty beings (jinn and humans).' Consequently, it is permissible to leave a wajib to repel hardship, but it is not tolerated to commit prohibited acts, especially major sins. From that is what al-Bazzazi mentioned in his Fatawa: Whoever cannot find a screen (to conceal himself) abandons istinja' (washing after relieving oneself), even on the bank of a river, because the prohibition (of exposing 'awra) outweighs the command (of istinja'), such that the prohibition encompasses all times and the command does not require repetition. (End). And the woman, if ghusl becomes obligatory upon her and she cannot find a screen from men, she postpones it. Unlike the man, if he cannot find a screen from men, he does not postpone it and performs ghusl. In istinja', if he cannot find a screen, he abandons it. The difference is that the ruling impurity (janabah) is stronger, and a woman among women is like a man among men. So in Sharh al-Nuqayah. Among its branches: exaggerating in rinsing the mouth and sniffing water (madmadah and istinshaq) is sunnah but disliked for the fasting person. Combing the hair is sunnah in purification but disliked for the muhrim. Sometimes benefit is considered because it outweighs the harm: from that is prayer when a condition of its conditions is corrupted, such as purity, covering, or facing the qiblah, for in all that there is harm because it involves deficiency in the majesty of Allah, such that one should not converse except in the most complete state. When something of that becomes impossible, prayer is permissible without it, giving precedence to the benefit of prayer over this harm. From it is lying: it is a prohibited harm, but when it involves bringing a benefit that outweighs it, it becomes permissible, like lying for reconciliation among people and to one's wife for her improvement. This type actually reverts to committing the lesser of two harms.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth major qa'idah: 'Repelling harms takes precedence over bringing benefits' (Dar' al-mafasid awla min jalb al-masalih). The author first states the maxim, then provides its evidentiary basis: the Hadith of commands and prohibitions, and a narration that abandoning a forbidden thing is better than worship. Because prohibitions are stronger, leaving a wajib (obligatory act like standing in prayer) is allowed to avoid hardship, but committing a sin is not excused for mere benefit. Several furu' are given: (1) If one cannot find a screen, he abandons istinja' (cleaning after relieving oneself) because exposing 'awra is prohibited and that prohibition outweighs the command to clean. (2) For ghusl (ritual bath), a woman postpones if no screen from men; a man does not postpone because his janabah is stronger and men among men are like women among women (though the text says women among men? Actually it says 'the woman among women is like a man among men', but the ruling differentiates: woman postpones, man does not). (3) Exaggerating in mouthwash and sniffing is sunnah but makruh for the fasting person (because it may break fast). (4) Combing hair is sunnah in wudu but makruh for the muhrim (because hair may fall). (5) Sometimes benefit overrides harm: if a necessary condition of prayer (like facing qiblah) is impossible, prayer is valid without it because the benefit of prayer outweighs the harm of the omission. (6) Lying is prohibited, but if it brings a greater benefit (like reconciliation), it becomes permissible—this is actually choosing the lesser harm. The passage integrates the maxim with its applications and exceptions.

Key Terms
المفسدة (al-mafsadah): Harm or evil; something that is prohibited or causes detriment.
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Benefit or good; something that brings reward or worldly good.
الاستنجاء (al-istinja'): Cleaning the private parts with water or stones after relieving oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth maxim, why is a woman required to postpone ghusl if she cannot find a screen from men?
A) Because ghusl is not obligatory for women.
B) Because the harm of exposing her 'awra outweighs the benefit of immediate ghusl.
C) Because she can perform tayammum instead.
D) Because men are not allowed to see women in any case.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Dar' al-mafasid awla min jalb al-masalih' is a foundational tool in Hanafi fatwa: avoid harm first, but when benefit clearly outweighs harm, the benefit is pursued.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Sixth Maxim: Need Takes the Place of Necessity – Foundations and Applications
Presentation of the sixth maxim with its evidence and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-hajah tunazzal manzilat al-darurah' to identify when a need-based concession applies in Hanafi mu'amalat, and distinguish between valid and invalid applications using the constraint of need genuineness.

Core Idea
Need permits what necessity permits, but only when the need is genuine and specific.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair introduces the sixth major qa'idah (legal maxim) in Hanafi jurisprudence: 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific.' The maxim allows certain contracts that would otherwise be invalid due to technical defects—such as gharar (uncertainty) or selling a non-existent item—if they fulfill a genuine public need. However, the maxim has limits: the need must be real and not create a greater harm. We will explore the maxim through its key examples and its practical application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the maxim and its justification
Read the maxim carefully: 'Need takes the place of necessity.' Understand that need (hajah) is a level below necessity (darurah) but still justifies legal concessions. The text gives examples of contracts permitted contrary to analogical reasoning (qiyas) because of need.
This maxim is a subsidiary of the major maxim 'al-mashaqqatu tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease).
2
Analyze the first furu': ijarah (leasing)
Ijarah is permitted despite renting a non-existent benefit because of general need. However, leasing a house for the benefits of another house is not allowed: the benefit is of the same type, so there is no need and the transaction resembles riba.
Contrast: ijarah of different benefits (e.g., a house for a car’s storage) would be permissible.
3
Examine daman al-darak (guarantee against defects)
This guarantee—where the seller promises to compensate if a defect appears—is allowed contrary to qiyas (because it guarantees an unknown). Need in commerce justifies it.
This is a classic example of the maxim in sales law.
4
Examine salam (advance sale)
Salam involves selling goods to be delivered later, with the price paid upfront. It is a sale of a non-existent item, normally prohibited, but permitted to meet the needs of the poor (or sellers) who require immediate liquidity.
Salam has detailed conditions to prevent abuse, but the basic permissibility stems from this maxim.
5
Identify the constraint (dabt) and exception (istithna')
The maxim is constrained by the principle that 'harm is removed as much as possible, not beyond.' The exception occurs when there is no genuine need—e.g., leasing a house for another house's identical benefit—then the original prohibition remains.
The mufti must always verify that the need is real and proportionate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : الحاجة تنزل منزلة الضرورة عامة كانت أو خاصَّة، ولهذا جوزت الإجارة على خلاف القياس للحاجة ولذا قلنا لا تجوز إجارة بيت بمنافع بيت لاتحاد جنس فلا حاجة بخلاف ما إذا اختلف. ومنها ضمان الدرك جوز على خلاف القياس. ومن ذلك جواز السلم على خلاف القياس لكونه بيع المعدوم دفعًا لحاجة المفاليس.
Sixth: Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific. For this reason, ijarah (leasing) was permitted contrary to analogy due to need. Therefore we say: leasing a house for the benefits of a house is not permitted because the type is one, so there is no need, unlike when they differ. And among them is daman al-darak (guarantee of defects) permitted contrary to analogy. And from that is the permissibility of salam (advance sale) contrary to analogy because it is the sale of a non-existent item, to address the need of the poor.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth major qa'idah: 'Need takes the place of necessity' (Al-hajah tunazzal manzilat al-darurah). The term 'hajah' refers to a general need that is not life-threatening but is significant enough to warrant a legal concession, while 'darurah' is a dire necessity. This maxim allows certain contracts that would otherwise be invalid due to technical defects (like gharar or non-existence) if they fulfill a common need. Examples: (1) Ijarah (leasing) is permitted even though it involves renting a non-existent benefit at the time of contract, because people need it. But leasing a house for the benefits of another house is not allowed because the benefit is identical, creating riba (interest) and no need is served. (2) Daman al-darak (a guarantee against defects) is allowed despite being a guarantee on an unknown, due to need in commerce. (3) Salam (advance payment for future delivery) is permitted even though it sells something not yet in existence, to help the needy who need cash immediately.

Key Terms
الحاجة (al-hajah): A need that is not life-threatening but causes significant hardship if unmet.
الضمان (ad-daman): Guarantee; here specifically daman al-darak: seller guarantees against defects.
السلم (as-salam): A sale where the price is paid upfront and the goods are delivered later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is salam (advance sale) permitted despite being a sale of non-existent goods?
A) Because it is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because it fulfills a need of the poor who need cash immediately.
C) Because all sales of non-existent items are permitted.
D) Because it is a form of charity.

Key Takeaway
The maxim allows concessions for genuine need, but the need must be specific and not create a greater harm.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Dar' al-Mafasid Awla min Jalb al-Masalih: The Fifth Major Maxim
Fifth legal maxim with applications and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Dar' al-mafasid awla min jalb al-masalih' to new furūʿ cases and identify the istithnā' where a dominant benefit overrides the default preference for warding off harm.

Core Idea
Warding off harm takes precedence over bringing benefit—but a clearly outweighing benefit may be chosen.

In this lesson, we explore the fifth major maxim of Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh: 'Warding off harm is preferable to bringing benefit.' This principle guides jurists when a benefit and a harm conflict: the default is to prevent the harm, even if that means forgoing a benefit. The author of al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓāʾir grounds this in the Prophet's hadith that prohibitions are absolute whereas commands are limited by capacity. Through several practical examples from ṭahārah, ṣawm, ḥajj, and ṣalāh, you will see how the maxim operates and when it is set aside for a greater good.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its foundational proof
Identify the exact Arabic wording of the maxim and the primary dalīl: the ḥadīth 'If I command you with something, do of it what you are able; if I forbid you from something, avoid it entirely.'
The ḥadīth shows the Sharīʿa's greater emphasis on prohibitions.
2
Analyze the first set of furūʿ: istinjāʾ and ghusl without privacy
Read the cases about a person without cover leaving istinjāʾ and a woman delaying ghusl. Note why the prohibition of exposure outweighs the command of purification.
The distinction between a woman and a man is based on the strength of ritual impurity and social context.
3
Examine furūʿ involving disliked acts in ṣawm and ḥajj
Consider the rulings on exaggeration in mouth rinsing for a fasting person and combing hair for a muḥrim. The harm of breaking fast or removing hair causes the act to become disliked despite being sunnah in other contexts.
The maxim applies even to acts that are normally recommended.
4
Recognize the exception when benefit outweighs harm
Identify the istithnāʾ: praying with missing conditions when impossible, and lying for reconciliation. Here the benefit (performing prayer, achieving harmony) is deemed greater, so the harm is tolerated.
This is not a contradiction; it is choosing the lesser harm.
5
Relate the maxim to other qawāʿid
Connect 'Dar' al-mafāsid' to 'al-Mashaqqa tajlib al-taysīr' and 'al-Ḍarar yuzāl'. Notice how they complement each other in facilitating rulings that prevent hardship.
These maxims together form a coherent system for ijtihād.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : ونظير القاعدة الرابعة قاعدة خامسة؛ وهي درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المصالح. فإذا تعارضت مفسدة ومصلحة قدَّم دفع المفسدة غالبا لأن اعتناء الشرع بالمنهيات أشد من اعتنائه بالمأمورات، ولذا قال عليه السلام: «إذا أمرتكم بشيء منه ما استطعتم وإذا نهيتكم عن شيءٍ فاجتنبوه» ورُوِيَ في الكشف حديثا لترك ذرة مما نهى الله عنه أفضل من عبادة «الثقلين» ومن ثم جاز ترك الواجب دفعا للمشقة، ولم يسامح في الإقدام على المنهيات خصوصا الكبائر. ومن ذلك ما ذكره البزازي في فتاواه : ومَن لم يجد سترة ترك الاستنجاء ولو على شط نهر لأن النهي راجح على الأمر حتى استوعب النهي الأزمان ولم يقتض الأمر التكرار. (انتهى). والمرأة إذا وجب عليها الغسل ولم تجد سترة من الرجال تؤخره، بخلاف الرجل إذا لم يجد سترة من الرجال لا يؤخره ويغتسل. وفي الاستنجاء إذا لم يجد سترة يتركه. والفرق أن النجاسة الحكمية أقوى والمرأة بين النساء كالرجل بين الرجال، كذا في شرح النقاية .ومن فروع ذلك المبالغة في المضمضة والاستنشاق مسنونة وتكره للصائم. وتخليل الشعر سُنّة في الطهارة ويُكره للمحرم. وقد تُراعى المصلحة لغلبتها على المفسدة؛ فمن ذلك الصلاة اختلال شرط من شروطها من الطهارة أو الستر أو مع الاستقبال فإن في كل ذلك مفسدة لما فيه من الإخلال بجلال الله تعالى في أن لا يناجي إلا على أكمل الأحوال ومتى تعذر عليه شيء من ذلك جازت الصلاة بدونه تقديمًا لمصلحة الصلاة على هذه المفسدة. ومنه الكذب مفسدة محرمة وهو متى تضمن جلب مصلحة تربو عليه جاز كالكذب للإصلاح بين الناس وعلى الزوجة لإصلاحها. وهذا النوع راجع إلى ارتكاب أخف المفسدتين في الحقيقة.
The fifth: An analogy to the fourth rule is the fifth rule: warding off harms is preferable to bringing benefits. When a harm and a benefit conflict, repelling harm is given precedence often because the Shari'a's concern with prohibitions is more intense than its concern with commands. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When I command you with something, do of it what you are able; and when I prohibit you from something, avoid it entirely.' It is also narrated in al-Kashf that leaving a speck of what Allah has prohibited is better than the worship of the two heavy ones (jinn and humans). Hence, it is permitted to leave an obligatory act to repel hardship, but one is not lenient in committing prohibitions, especially major sins. Among its applications is what al-Bazzazi mentioned in his Fatawa: Whoever does not find a cover (for privacy) leaves off istinja' (cleansing after toilet) even on a riverbank, because prohibition outweighs command to the extent that prohibition encompasses all times and command does not necessitate repetition. Similarly, a woman who is obligated to perform ghusl and does not find a cover from men delays it, unlike a man who if he does not find a cover from men does not delay and performs ghusl. And for istinja, if he does not find a cover, he leaves it. The difference is that ritual impurity is stronger and a woman among women is like a man among men, as stated in Sharh al-Niqaya. Among its applications: exaggeration in rinsing the mouth and sniffing water is sunna but disliked for a fasting person. Combing the hair is sunna in purification but disliked for a pilgrim in ihram. Sometimes benefit is considered because it outweighs harm; from that is prayer when a condition like purity, covering, or facing the qibla is defective, for all that contains harm due to compromising the majesty of Allah in that one should not converse with Him except in the most perfect state. When something of that is not possible, prayer is permitted without it, giving precedence to the benefit of prayer over this harm. From it is lying, which is a prohibited harm, but when it involves bringing a benefit that outweighs it, it is permitted, like lying for reconciliation between people or to a wife to set matters right. This kind essentially resorts to committing the lesser of two harms.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth major legal maxim: 'Warding off harm is preferable to bringing benefit.' The author begins by situating this maxim as analogous to the fourth rule, then provides the primary proof: the Prophet's hadith indicating that prohibitions are absolute while commands are limited by capacity. The underlying reasoning is that the Shari'a gives greater weight to avoiding sin than to performing good deeds. Several applications illustrate the maxim: a person without privacy may omit istinja because the prohibition of exposing oneself outweighs the command to cleanse; a woman delays obligatory ghusl if she lacks cover from men, while a man does not delay because his covering is simpler. The difference is explained by the type of impurity. Further applications include the dislike of exaggerating oral rinsing for a fasting person (due to risk of breaking fast) and combing hair for a pilgrim in ihram (due to prohibition of hair removal). The passage also notes exceptions: when benefit clearly outweighs harm, such as praying without full purity or covering when impossible, or lying to bring about reconciliation. These are all instances of choosing the lesser harm.

Key Terms
مفسدة (mafsada): A harm or evil that the Shari'a seeks to prevent
مصلحة (maslaha): A benefit or good that the Shari'a seeks to achieve
درء المفاسد (dar' al-mafasid): The principle of warding off harms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth maxim, what is the ruling for a fasting person regarding exaggeration in mouth rinsing?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is recommended
C) It is disliked
D) It is prohibited

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Warding off harm is preferable to bringing benefit' is a default presumption, not an absolute rule; when the benefit clearly outweighs, the harm may be tolerated as a lesser evil.

Custom is Authoritative: The Sixth Major Maxim
Sixth major maxim: custom as a source of law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of custom (urf) as defined by Hanafi jurists and apply the maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah' to determine rulings in cases where custom is authoritative.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative — but only where no explicit Shari‘a text exists.

This lesson unpacks the sixth major maxim of Hanafi fiqh: 'Custom is authoritative.' The maxim is not a prophetic hadith but a statement of Ibn Mas‘ud (ra), yet it forms a cornerstone of Hanafi legal reasoning. You will learn the evidence for this maxim, its classification into three types of custom, and how it is applied to concrete rulings like the definition of flowing water and the determination of a woman's habitual menstruation period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Read the opening of the passage: 'The sixth maxim: Custom is authoritative.' Note that the author traces it to a saying of Ibn Mas‘ud (mawquf), not a prophetic hadith. This is important for understanding its epistemological weight.
Even without prophetic status, the maxim is firmly established through widespread acceptance and application.
2
Understand the scope of custom (three types)
Study the classification: general custom (e.g., putting a foot forward), specific custom (e.g., grammatical terminology), and legal custom (e.g., Shari‘a terms for salat, zakat). Recognize that each type operates in different domains.
Legal custom (al-‘urfiyya al-shar‘iyya) overrides the literal linguistic meaning, as with the word 'salat' shifting from 'prayer' to a specific ritual.
3
Analyze the application rulings (furuat)
Work through each derived ruling: definition of flowing water, fall of a large sea animal, large quantity of water, menstruation habit, and action nullifying prayer. For each, determine how custom provides the criterion.
In each case, the fixed textual measure is replaced by what people customarily recognize.
4
Recognize the limitation (istithna')
The maxim does not apply when there is an explicit Shari‘a text. Custom yields to nass. This is a critical boundary condition.
The governing maxim here is 'النصوص مقدمة على العرف' (Texts take precedence over custom).
5
Apply the maxim to new cases
As a mufti, when encountering a novel practice, first check for an explicit text. If none exists, assess if the practice is consistent, accepted, and not contradictory. If so, apply the maxim to validate it.
This is the practical skill of tatbiq (application) that the lesson aims to build.

القاعدة السادسة : العادة محكمة وأصلها قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما رآه المسلمون حسنًا فهو عند الله حسن» قال العلائي: لم أجده مرفوعًا في شيء من كتب الحديث أصلاً ولا بسند ضعيف بعد طول البحث وكثرة الكشف والسؤال، وإنما هو من قول عبد الله مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه موقوفا عليه أخرجه أحمد في مسنده. واعلم أن اعتبار العادة والعُرف يرجع إليه في الفقه في مسائل كثيرة حتى جعلوا ذلك أصلاً، فقالوا في الأصول في باب ما تترك به الحقيقة: تترك الحقيقة بدلالة الاستعمال والعادة، كذا ذكر فخر الإسلام فاختلف في عطف العادة على الاستعمال فقيل هما مترادفان وقيل المراد من الاستعمال نقل اللفظ عن موضوعه الأصلي إلى معناه المجازي شرعًا وغلبة استعماله فيه ومن العادة نقله إلى معناه المجازي عُرْفًا، وتمامه في الكشف الكبير . وذكر الهندي في شرح المغني : العادة عبارة عما يستقر في النفوس من الأمور المتكررة المقبولة عند الطباع السليمة وهي أنواع ثلاثة : العُرفية العامة، كوضع القدم، والعرفية الخاصة كاصطلاح كل طائفة مخصوصة كالرفع للنحاة، والفرق والجمع والنقض للنظار. والعُرفية الشرعية كالصلاة والزكاة والحج تركت معانيها اللغوية بمعانيها الشرعية. The sixth maxim: Custom is authoritative (al-‘ādah muḥakkamah). Its origin is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'What the Muslims see as good is good in the sight of Allah.' Al-‘Ala’i said: I did not find it as a marfu‘ hadith in any hadith book at all, not even with a weak chain, after extensive research, investigation, and inquiry; rather it is the saying of ‘Abdullah ibn Mas‘ud (may Allah be pleased with him) as a mawquf narration, recorded by Ahmad in his Musnad. Know that reliance on custom (al-‘ādah) and ‘urf is referred to in fiqh in many issues, so much so that they made it a principle. They said in uṣūl, in the chapter on when the literal meaning is abandoned: the literal meaning is abandoned by evidence of usage (isti‘mal) and custom (al-‘ādah). Thus Fakhr al-Islam mentioned it. There is a difference of opinion in connecting custom to usage: some say they are synonymous; others say that usage refers to transferring a word from its original meaning to a metaphorical meaning by Shari‘a and its predominant use, and custom refers to transferring it to a metaphorical meaning by customary practice (urf). Its full detail is in al-Kashf al-Kabir. Al-Hindi mentioned in Sharh al-Mughni: Custom is what settles in the souls of repeated, acceptable matters among sound natures. It is of three types: general customary (‘urfiya ‘amma), like putting the foot forward; specific customary (‘urfiya khassa), like the terminology of each particular group, such as raf‘ for grammarians and difference, combination, and abrogation for theologians; and legal customary (‘urfiya shar‘iyya), like prayer, zakat, hajj, which have left their linguistic meanings for legal meanings. (end). Among the applications derived from this maxim: the definition of flowing water – the correct view is that it is what people consider flowing; the fall of a large sea animal into a well – the correct view is that large is not what the observer deems abundant; the definition of a large amount of water that is like flowing water – the correct view is to defer to the opinion of the person concerned, not to estimate it by something like a tenth of a tenth and the like; and regarding menstruation and postpartum bleeding, they said: if the blood exceeds the maximum of menstruation or postpartum, it is referred back to the days of her habit. And from that, the action that nullifies prayer is delegated to...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth major maxim: 'Custom is authoritative' (العادة محكمة). The author first cites the apparent hadith basis, but notes that the hadith is actually a mawquf saying of Ibn Mas‘ud, not a prophetic statement. Nevertheless, the principle is firmly established in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim is explained in terms of usul al-fiqh: when a word is ambiguous, its meaning is determined by common usage and custom. The passage cites a disagreement whether 'usage' and 'custom' are synonymous or distinct; the latter view distinguishes between established technical usage (e.g., Shari‘a terms) and customary usage (urf). The passage then classifies custom into three types: general (like putting one foot forward in walking), specific (like technical terms of scholars), and legal (like the Shari‘a meanings of salat, zakat, hajj). Finally, several rulings are derived from this maxim: the definition of 'flowing water' is whatever people customarily consider flowing; a 'large' sea animal falling into a well is determined by the observer's opinion, not a fixed measurement; a woman's menstruation habit is determined by her previous custom; and actions that nullify prayer are determined by custom (the text cuts off at this point). The underlying principle is that custom can override literal definitions and standard rulings when it is consistent and accepted.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘adah): Customary practice that settles in souls due to repetition and acceptance
العرف (urf): Custom, often used interchangeably with al-‘adah, but some distinguish them
العرفية العامة (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma): General custom, common to all people regardless of specialization
العرفية الخاصة (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa): Specific custom, particular to a group like scholars
العرفية الشرعية (al-‘urfiyya al-shar‘iyya): Legal custom, where Shari‘a terms override linguistic meanings

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Water that is at least one-tenth of a tenth of a measure

Key Takeaway
Custom is authoritative in Hanafi fiqh only where no explicit Shari‘a text exists, and it is classified into general, specific, and legal custom.
