Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
12 min
The Sixth Major Maxim: Custom is Authoritative
Establishes the maxim with evidence, definition, and applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah', identify its evidential basis (the mawquf saying of Ibn Mas‘ud), and apply it to furu‘ cases including the exception when a text exists.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative — but only where no text overrides it.

This lesson introduces the sixth major legal maxim of the Hanafi school: custom is authoritative (العادة محكمة). This principle governs many aspects of fiqh, from defining water to fasting and interpreting contracts. You will learn the maxim's evidential basis, its definition and types, its broad range of applications, and its critical limit: custom yields to explicit textual evidence (nass).

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its evidence
Begin by memorising the maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah'. Identify its proof: the mawquf narration from ‘Abdullāh ibn Mas‘ūd: 'What the Muslims see as good is good with Allah.'
Though often cited as a hadith, the author clarifies it is a companion saying, but still authoritative in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the definition and categories of custom
Define custom as repeated matters accepted by sound natures. Distinguish three types: general, special, and Shari‘ah custom.
General custom (e.g., walking posture), special custom (e.g., technical terms of a discipline), and Shari‘ah custom (e.g., transferring words like salah to their ritual meaning).
3
Examine the furu‘ (branches) illustrating the maxim
Work through the listed applications: flowing water, water in wells, menstruation, actions in prayer, fallen fruits, hiring, ribawi items, fasting on the day of doubt, gifts to a judge, and interpretation of waqf/vows/wills/oaths.
In each case, note how custom determines the ruling when no explicit text exists.
4
Identify the exception: custom defers to nass
For ribawi items where the Shari‘ah has specified volume or weight, custom is not considered according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. This establishes the rule that custom operates only in the absence of a definitive text.
Abu Yusuf differed, but the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi in internal ikhtilaf
In practice, the mufti must ascertain local custom, but where a clear nass exists (e.g., specified measures for ribawi goods), the nass prevails. This is the relied-upon position.
The fatwa hierarchy prioritises nass over custom, as stressed in Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة : العادة محكمة
وأصلها قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما رآه المسلمون حسنًا فهو عند الله حسن» (۱) قال العلائي: لم أجده مرفوعًا في شيء من كتب الحديث أصلاً ولا بسند ضعيف بعد طول البحث وكثرة الكشف والسؤال، وإنما هو من قول عبد الله مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه موقوفا عليه أخرجه أحمد في مسنده.
واعلم أن اعتبار العادة والعُزف يرجع إليه في الفقه في مسائل كثيرة حتى جعلوا ذلك أصلاً، فقالوا في الأصول في باب ما تترك به الحقيقة: تترك الحقيقة بدلالة الاستعمال والعادة، كذا ذكر فخر الإسلام فاختلف في عطف العادة على الاستعمال فقيل هما مترادفان وقيل المراد من الاستعمال نقل اللفظ عن موضوعه الأصلي إلى معناه المجازي شرعًا وغلبة استعماله فيه ومن العادة نقله إلى معناه المجازي عُرْفًا، وتمامه في الكشف الكبير .
وذكر الهندي في شرح المغني : العادة عبارة عما يستقر في النفوس من الأمور المتكررة المقبولة عند الطباع السليمة وهي أنواع ثلاثة : العُرفية العامة، كوضع القدم، والعرفية الخاصة كاصطلاح كل طائفة مخصوصة كالرفع للنحاة، والفرق والجمع والنقض للنظار. والعُرفية ،الشرعية كالصلاة والزكاة والحج تركت معانيها اللغوية بمعانيها الشرعية. (انتهى).
فمما فرع على هذه القاعدة حدّ الماء الجاري، الأصح أنه ما يعده الناس جاريًا، ومنها وقوع البحر الكثير في البئر ؛ الأصح أن الكثير لا يستكثره الناظر. ومنها حد الماء الكثير الملحق بالجاري، الأصح تفويضه إلى رأي المبتلى به لا التقدير بشيء من العشر في العشر ونحوه ومنها الحيض والنفاس قالوا لو زاد الدم على أكثر الحيض والنفاس يرد إلى أيام .عادتها ومن ذلك العمل المفسد للصلاة مفوض إلى العُرْف لو كان بحيث لو رآه راء يظن أنه خارج الصلاة ومنها تناول الثمار الساقطة وفي إجارة الظئر ) وفيما لا نص فيه من الأموال الربوية يعتبر فيه العزف في كونه كيليا أو وزنيًا. وأما المنصوص على كيله أو وزنه فلا اعتبار بالعُزف فيه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله خلافًا لأبي يوسف رحمه الله . وقوَّاه في فتح القدير من باب الربا ولا خصوصية للرّبا، وإنما العُزف غير معتبر في المنصوص عليه، قال في الظهيرية من الصلاة وكان محمد بن الفضل يقول السِّرَّة إلى موضع نبات الشعر من العانة ليست بعورة لتعامل العمال في الإبداء عن ذلك الموضع عند الاتزار، وفي النزع عند العادة الظاهرة نوع حرج. وهذا ضعيف وبعيد لأن التعامل بخلاف النص لا يعتبر. (انتهى بلفظه) .
وفي صوم يوم الشك فلا يُكرَه لمَن له عادة وكذا صوم يومين قبله. والمذهب عدم كراهية صومه بنية النقل مطلقا.
ومنها قبول الهدية للقاضي ممَّن له عادة بالإهداء له قبل توليته بشرط ألاً على العادة، فإن زاد عليها ردَّ الزائد والأكل من الطعام المُقَدَّم له ضيافة بلا صريح الإذن .
ومنها ألفاظ الواقفين تُبتنى على عُرفهم كما في وقف فتح القدير، وكذا لفظ الناذر والموصي والحالف، وكذا الأقارير تُبتَنى عليه إلا فيما نذكره وسيأتي في مسائل الأيمان .
(۱) رواه أحمد في مسنده (۳۷۹/۱).
The sixth principle: Custom is authoritative. Its origin is his statement, peace be upon him: 'What the Muslims see as good is good with Allah.' Al-‘Ala’i said: I did not find it as a marfu‘ hadith in any hadith book at all, not even with a weak chain after lengthy search and much investigation and inquiry; rather it is from the statement of ‘Abdullah ibn Mas‘ud, may Allah be pleased with him, as a mawquf narration, recorded by Ahmad in his Musnad. Know that consideration of custom and usage is referred to in fiqh in many issues, so much so that they made it a principle. They said in usul, in the chapter on when the literal meaning is abandoned: the literal meaning is abandoned by indication of usage and custom. Thus mentioned Fakhr al-Islam. Then there is disagreement on the conjunction of custom with usage: some say they are synonymous, and some say that by usage is meant transfer of a word from its original meaning to a metaphorical meaning by Shari‘ah and its predominant use, and by custom is meant transfer to a metaphorical meaning by common practice. The rest is in al-Kashf al-Kabir. Al-Hindi mentioned in Sharh al-Mughni: Custom is an expression for what settles in souls from repeated matters accepted by sound natures. It has three types: general customary, like putting the foot; special customary, like the terminology of each specific group, such as raising for grammarians, and difference, conjunction, and criticism for theologians; and Shari‘ah customary, like prayer, zakat, and hajj, which left their linguistic meanings for their Shari‘ah meanings. (End). Among the branches on this principle: the definition of flowing water—the correct view is that it is what people consider flowing; and among them: a large sea falling into a well—the correct view is that a large amount does not make the observer consider it abundant; and among them: the definition of a large amount of water considered as flowing—the correct view is to refer it to the opinion of the person affected, not to estimate it by a tenth in a tenth and the like; and among them: menstruation and postpartum bleeding—they said if the blood exceeds the maximum of menstruation and postpartum, it is referred to the days of her habit; and among them: actions that invalidate prayer are referred to custom—if it is such that if a person saw it he would think he is outside prayer; and among them: picking up fallen fruits; and in hiring a wet nurse; and for wealth not mentioned in texts regarding ribawi items, custom is considered in whether it is measured by volume or weight. As for what is specified by text regarding its volume or weight, custom is not considered according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, contrary to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. And he strengthened it in Fath al-Qadir in the chapter of riba, and there is no specificity for riba; rather custom is not considered in what is specified by text. It is said in al-Zahiriyyah from the chapter of prayer: Muhammad ibn al-Fadl used to say that the navel to the area of pubic hair growth is not ‘awrah due to the practice of workers in exposing that area when wearing an izār, and in removing it due to apparent custom there is some hardship. This is weak and far-fetched because practice contrary to text is not considered. (End of his wording). And regarding fasting on the day of doubt, it is not disliked for one who has a habit, and likewise fasting two days before. The madhhab is that fasting it with the intention of nafl is absolutely not disliked. And among them: acceptance of gifts by a judge from one who had a habit of gifting before his appointment, provided he does not increase the habit; if he increases, he returns the excess. And eating food presented to him as hospitality without explicit permission. And among them: the expressions of waqf founders are built on their custom, as in the waqf in Fath al-Qadir, and likewise the expression of the one making a vow, the testator, and the swearer; and likewise acknowledgments are built upon it except what we will mention, and it will come in the chapters of oaths. (1) Narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad (1/379).
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth major legal maxim: 'Custom is authoritative.' The author begins by stating the maxim, then provides its foundational evidence—a hadith that is actually a saying of Ibn Mas‘ud (mawquf), not a marfu‘ hadith as commonly assumed. He then explains that custom and usage ('urf) are key in fiqh, even used to abandon literal meanings. The definition of custom is given: repeated matters accepted by sound natures, divided into general, special, and Shari‘ah customs. The bulk of the passage lists numerous applications (furu‘) of this maxim across various chapters of fiqh: defining flowing water, water in wells, menstruation, actions invalidating prayer, picking up fruit, hiring wet nurses, ribawi items (where custom determines measure unless text specifies), fasting on the day of doubt, gifts to judges, and interpretation of waqf, vows, wills, oaths, and acknowledgments. The author also notes exceptions: where a text exists, custom is not considered according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, though Abu Yusuf differed. The practical import is that many rulings depend on prevalent custom, and the mufti should ascertain local custom.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘ādah): Custom: what settles in souls from repeated matters accepted by sound natures, including general, special, and Shari‘ah customs.
العرف (al-‘urf): Usage: often synonymous with custom, but some distinguish as transfer of meaning by predominant use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Custom is a powerful source in Hanafi fiqh, but it yields to explicit textual evidence (nass).

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

The Principle of Custom and Its Applications
Presents the maxim of 'urf and its various furuat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' to identify when custom is authoritative and when it is overridden by an explicit text (nass), and to evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding custom in ribawi items.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative only in the absence of an explicit text; nass overrides custom.

This passage from al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir presents the major qāʿidah 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah' (custom is authoritative) and illustrates it with seven practical applications (furūʿ) across prayer, sales, hiring, ribā, fasting, judiciary, and waqf/vows/oaths. The author also addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute on whether custom can override an explicit textual specification of weight or volume for ribawi items. This lesson will guide you through the maxim, its scope, its exceptions, and the practical consequences of the internal disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its authority
Identify the qāʿidah kubrā (major maxim) 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah' and its rank. Understand that it applies across all fiqh chapters except where contradicted by an explicit text (nass).
The maxim is a kullīyah (universal) rule.
2
List the seven furūʿ (applications) from the passage
Go through each application: (1) actions in prayer that appear external, (2) taking fallen fruits, (3) hiring a wet nurse, (4) determining weight/volume of non-textually specified ribawi items, (5) fasting on the day of doubt, (6) gifts to a judge, (7) interpretation of waqf, vow, will, oath, and acknowledgment expressions. For each, note the ruling and the fiqh chapter.
These demonstrate the maxim's breadth.
3
Analyze the key exception: nass overrides custom
Focus on the ribawi example: when an explicit text specifies weight or volume, custom is not considered. This is the main exception to the maxim. Note the dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that nass overrides; Abu Yusuf argues custom can still prevail.
This exception is the dabt (restriction) of the maxim.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Yusuf vs. the two imams)
State the positions, proofs, and the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence). Use the madhhab_dispute_map to clarify the reasoning. Understand why the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon (mufta bihi).
The dispute is legitimate ikhtilaf, resolved by tarjīḥ.
5
Apply the maxim to a mufti's case
Role-play as a mufti: Given a modern query (e.g., determining whether a new commodity is measured by weight or volume for ribā purposes), decide whether to rely on custom or search for a nass. State the fatwa, citing the authority level and tarjīḥ.
This step bridges theory to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) العُرْف لو كان بحيث لو رآه راء يظن أنه خارج الصلاة ومنها تناول الثمار الساقطة وفي إجارة الظئر وفيما لا نص فيه من الأموال الربوية يعتبر فيه العرف في كونه كيليا أو وزنيًا. وأما المنصوص على كيله أو وزنه فلا اعتبار بالعرف فيه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله خلافًا لأبي يوسف رحمه الله . وقوَّاه في فتح القدير من باب الربا ولا خصوصية للرّبا، وإنما العرف غير معتبر في المنصوص عليه، قال في الظهيرية من الصلاة وكان محمد بن الفضل يقول السِّرَّة إلى موضع نبات الشعر من العانة ليست بعورة لتعامل العمال في الإبداء عن ذلك الموضع عند الاتزار، وفي النزع عند العادة الظاهرة نوع حرج. وهذا ضعيف وبعيد لأن التعامل بخلاف النص لا يعتبر. (انتهى بلفظه) . وفي صوم يوم الشك فلا يُكرَه لمَن له عادة وكذا صوم يومين قبله. والمذهب عدم كراهية صومه بنية النقل مطلقا. ومنها قبول الهدية للقاضي ممَّن له عادة بالإهداء له قبل توليته بشرط ألا على العادة، فإن زاد عليها ردَّ الزائد والأكل من الطعام المُقَدَّم له ضيافة بلا صريح الإذن . ومنها ألفاظ الواقفين تُبتنى على عُرفهم كما في وقف فتح القدير، وكذا لفظ الناذر والموصي والحالف، وكذا الأقارير تُبتَنى عليه إلا فيما نذكره وسيأتي في مسائل الأيمان .
(1) Custom: if it is such that if a person saw it, he would think that he is outside the prayer. And from them is taking fallen fruits. And in the ijarah of a wet nurse. And regarding wealth that is subject to riba for which there is no text, custom is considered in whether it is measured by weight or volume. As for what is textually specified as measured by weight or volume, custom is not considered in it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), contrary to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him). And it was strengthened in Fath al-Qadir from the chapter of riba, and there is no specificity to riba; rather custom is not considered in what is textually specified. He said in al-Zahira from the prayer chapter: Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: the navel to the place of growth of pubic hair is not 'awrah due to the workers' practice of exposing that area when wearing the izar, and in removing it due to prevalent custom there is some difficulty. This is weak and far-fetched because practice contrary to text is not considered. (End of his wording). And regarding fasting on the day of doubt: it is not disliked for one who has a custom, and likewise fasting two days before it. The madhhab is that it is absolutely not disliked to fast with the intention of nafl. And from them is accepting a gift for a judge from one who had a custom of gifting to him before his appointment, on condition that it does not exceed the custom; if he increases, he returns the excess. And eating from food presented to him as hospitality without explicit permission. And from them: the expressions of endowers are built upon their custom, as in the waqf of Fath al-Qadir, and likewise the expressions of the one making a vow, the testator, the one swearing an oath, and likewise acknowledgments are built upon it except what we will mention, and it will come in the chapters of oaths.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion on the legal maxim 'al-'ādah muḥakkamah' (custom is authoritative). It begins by enumerating several furuat (applications) of this principle across various fiqh chapters. The first example concerns actions in prayer that, by prevalent custom, would make an onlooker think the person is no longer in prayer—such actions are considered to nullify prayer if they conflict with the prayer's form. The second is taking fallen fruits, which is permissible under certain customary conditions. The third is the hire of a wet nurse, where custom determines default terms. Importantly, in ribawi items, if there is no explicit textual ruling (nass) on whether they are measured by weight or volume, custom determines it. But if the text specifies, custom is overridden according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf considers custom even in textually specified cases. The passage then discusses a weak opinion about the 'awrah of the navel area based on customary exposure of workers; this is rejected because practice cannot override explicit nass. Next, fasting on the day of doubt: it is not disliked if one has a custom of fasting (e.g., regularly fasting on Mondays that coincide with the day of doubt). The ruling on gifts to a judge: if the donor had a custom of gifting before the judge's appointment, the judge may accept up to that customary amount, rejecting any excess. Finally, the wording of endowers, vow-makers, testators, and oath-takers is interpreted according to their custom, as is acknowledgment, except where otherwise noted in the chapter on oaths.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice that is prevalent and constant among people, used as a basis for rulings when no explicit text exists.
العادة (al-'ādah): Recurring practice that becomes normative in a community.
النص (al-nass): Explicit textual ruling from Qur'an or Hadith that overrides custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling regarding custom in ribawi items that have an explicit textual specification of weight or volume?
A) Custom is considered and overrides the text.
B) Custom is considered only if it is prevalent and does not cause hardship.
C) Custom is not considered because the text is explicit.
D) Custom is considered only for weight but not volume.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-ʿādah muḥakkamah' is a powerful tool for a mufti, but it yields to explicit nass; custom fills the gaps where text is silent.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
12 min
Custom is Considered When Consistent or Prevalent
General rule on when custom is considered, followed by multiple furu' applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when custom is legally considered and apply it to at least three furu' from different fiqh chapters.

Core Idea
Custom is only a source of law when it is consistent (mut'tarid) or prevalent (ghalib); rare or exceptional practices carry no legal weight.

In Hanafi fiqh, custom ('urf or 'adah) is a subsidiary source of law, but it is not automatically binding. This lesson unpacks the foundational maxim that custom is only considered when it is consistent or prevalent. By examining a series of furu' from sales, hiring, endowments, and education, you will see how jurists operationalized this rule, resolving ambiguities in contracts and institutional arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custom in question
Read each furu' and determine what practice is being invoked as a custom. Specify the context: e.g., currency, payment schedule, supply costs, disposal of leftovers, holiday pay.
Custom can be explicit ('urf qawli) or tacit ('urf fi'li).
2
Assess consistency (ittirad) or prevalence (ghalabah)
For each custom, decide whether it is continuous and regular (mut'tarid) or predominant in that locality (ghalib). If neither, the custom is not considered.
The source often assumes the custom is prevalent; you must test this condition.
3
Apply the qawida to derive the ruling
Once the custom qualifies, apply it to fill the contractual gap. The ruling is that the absolute expression is understood according to the custom.
The related qawida 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka'l-mashrut shartan' complements this.
4
Note exceptions and disagreements
Some furu' have intra-Hanafi differences (e.g., teacher holiday pay). Identify the various opinions and the tarjih (preferred view) in later fatwa practice.
The teacher holiday case shows evolution: early jurists differed, but later custom becomes decisive.
5
Cross-reference with related qawa'id
Consider how this maxim interacts with other principles like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' or 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir'. Custom often overrides literal interpretation.
In the teacher case, the intention behind holiday (rest vs. review) may conflict, but custom resolves it.

Source Text

المبحث الثاني: إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطّردت أو غلبت؛ ولذا قالوا في البيع لو باع بدراهم أو دنانير وكانا في بلد اختلف فيه النقود مع الاختلاف في المالية والرواج انصرف البيع إلى الأغلب قال في الهداية : لأنه هو المتعارف فينصرف المطلق إليه.
·
ومنها لو باع التاجر في السوق شيئًا بثمن ولم يصرحا بحلول ولا تأجيل وكان المتعارف فيما بينهم أن البائع يأخذ كل جمعة قدرًا معلوما انصرف إليه بلا بيان قالوا لأن المعروف كالمشروط ولكن إذا باعه المشتري تولية ولم يبين التقسيط للمشتري هل يكون للمشتري الخيار؟ فمنهم من أثبته الجمهور على أنه يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان لكونه حالاً بالعقد، ذكره الزيلعي في التولية.
ومنها في استئجار الكاتب قالوا الحبر عليه والأقلام والخياط قالوا الخيط هذا والإبرة عليه عملاً بالعزف وينبغي أن يكون الكحل على الكحال للعزف، ومن القبيل طعام العبد فإنه على المستأجر بخلاف علف الدابَّة فإنه على المؤجر حتى لو شرط على المستأجر فسدَّت كما في البزازية بخلاف استئجار الظئر بطعامها وكسوتها فإنه جائز وإن كان مجهولاً للعُزف وتفرّع على أن علف الدابة على مالكها دون المستأجر أن المستأجر لو تركها بلا علف حتى مانت جوعًا لم يضمن كما في البزازية .
·
ومنها ما في وقف القنية : بعث شمعًا في شهر رمضان إلى مسجد فاحترق وبقي منه ثلثه أو دونه ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذه بغير إذن الدافع، ولو كان العُزف في ذلك الموضع أن الإمام أو المؤذن يأخذه من غير صريح الإذن في ذلك كان له ذلك . (انتهى).
ومنها البطالة في المدارس كأيام الأعياد ويوم عاشوراء وشهر رمضان في درس الفقه لم أرها صريحة في كلامهم والمسألة على وجهين فإن كانت مشروطة لم يسقط من المعلوم شيء وإلا فينبغي أن يلحق ببطالة القاضي.
وقد أخذوا في أخذ القاضي ما رتب من بيت المال في يوم بطالته، فقال في المحيط إنه يأخذ في يوم البطالة لأنه يستريح لليوم الثاني وقيل لا يأخذ. (انتهى). وفي المنية : القاضي يستحق الكفاية من بيت المال في يوم البطالة في الأصح واختاره في منظومة ابن وهبان وقال إنه الأظهر فينبغي أن يكون كذلك في المدارس لأن يوم البطالة للاستراحة وفي الحقيقة يكون للمطالعة والتحرير عند ذي الهمة ولكن تعارف الفقهاء في زماننا بطالة طويلة أدت إلى أن صار الغالب البطالة وأيام التدريس قليلة، وبعض المدرّسين يتقدَّم في أخذ المعلوم على غيره محتجا بأن المدرس من الشعائر مستدلاً بما في الحاوي القدسي، مع أن ما في الحاوي القدسي إنما هو في المدرس للمدرسة لا في كل مدرس، فخرج مدرّس المسجد كما هو في مصر، والفرق بينهما أن المدرسة تتعطل إذا غاب المدرّس بحيث تتعطّل أصلاً بخلاف المسجد فإنه لا يتعطّل لغيبة المدرس.
Second investigation: Custom is only considered if it is consistent or prevalent. Hence they said in sale: if one sells for dirhams or dinars in a city where there are different currencies with difference in value and circulation, the sale is referred to the most common. Al-Hidayah said: because it is the customary, so the absolute is referred to it. Among them: if a trader sells something in the market for a price and they do not specify immediate payment or deferment, and the custom among them is that the seller takes a known amount every Friday, it is referred to that without specification. They said because the known is like the stipulated. But if the buyer sells it as a partnership without explaining the installment to the buyer, does the buyer have an option? Some affirmed it; the majority hold that he sells it as a murabaha without explanation because it is cash by contract, mentioned by al-Zayla'i in al-Tawliya. Among them: in hiring a scribe, they said the ink and pen are on him; and a tailor, the thread and needle are on him, acting by custom. Similarly, kohl should be on the kohl-applier due to custom. From this category is the food of a slave, which is on the hirer, unlike the fodder of an animal, which is on the owner, so if he stipulates it on the hirer, it is invalid, as in al-Bazzaziyyah, unlike hiring a wet nurse with her food and clothing, which is valid even if unknown due to custom. A branch: since fodder is on the owner, if the hirer leaves it without fodder until it dies of hunger, he is not liable, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. Among them: in Waqf al-Qunyah: one sent candles in Ramadan to a mosque, they were burned and a third or less remained; it is not for the imam or muezzin to take it without permission of the donor. If the custom in that place is that the imam or muezzin takes it without explicit permission, he may do so. (End). Among them: the holiday (بطالة) in schools like days of festivals, Ashura, and Ramadan in the lesson of fiqh: I have not seen explicit mention in their speech, and the issue is two-fold: if it is stipulated, nothing falls from the salary; otherwise, it should be attached to the judge's holiday. And they have taken that the judge takes what is allocated from the treasury on his holiday day. Al-Muhit says he takes on the holiday day because he rests for the next day; and it was said he does not take. (End). In al-Munyah: the judge deserves the sufficiency from the treasury on the holiday day in the most correct opinion, chosen by Ibn Wahban in his poem, and he said it is the most apparent. So it should be similar in schools because the holiday day is for rest, and in reality it is for review and writing for the industrious. But the custom of jurists in our time has become long holidays such that holidays are predominant and teaching days are few. Some teachers take advance payment arguing that the teacher is among the symbols of Islam, relying on what is in al-Hawi al-Qudsi, with the proviso that what is in al-Hawi al-Qudsi is about the teacher of the school only, not every teacher. Thus the teacher of a mosque, as in Egypt, is excluded. The difference between them is that the school becomes idle if the teacher is absent such that it is completely idle, unlike the mosque, which is not idle due to the teacher's absence.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi principle regarding custom (العادة). It begins by stating that custom is only legally relevant when it is continuous (اطراد) or predominant (غلبة). The author then provides several applications (furu') from various chapters of fiqh to illustrate how this principle governs actual rulings. The first example concerns a sale where the currency is ambiguous; the ruling refers to the most common currency due to custom. The second example involves a trader's sale with no explicit payment schedule; the custom of periodic payments is enforced. The third example discusses hiring professionals (scribe, tailor, kohl-applier) where custom determines who bears the cost of supplies. The fourth example deals with leftover candles in a mosque: custom may permit the imam or muezzin to take them without explicit permission. The fifth and most extensive example examines the issue of holiday leave for teachers in schools, analogizing to the judge's holiday pay. The author weighs different opinions and notes the contemporary practice of long holidays.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'adah): Custom; established practice that is consistent or prevalent
اطراد (it'tirad): Consistency; the custom being continuous and regular
غلبة (ghalabah): Prevalence; the custom being predominant in practice

Custom Overrides Sharia Terminology in Oaths
Legal maxim with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Custom of usage is given precedence, especially in oaths' to determine whether an oath is broken in various scenarios, and identify the exceptions where sharia definitions prevail.

Core Idea
In oaths, what people commonly mean by a word matters more than its Quranic or technical definition.

The tension between literal scriptural language and everyday usage is a recurring theme in Hanafi fiqh. This maxim resolves that tension in oaths by prioritizing custom ('urf) over the formal shar'i meaning. The passage demonstrates this with vivid examples and then carves out exceptions where sharia insists on its own definitions.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Start by committing the rule to memory: 'Custom of usage is given precedence, especially in oaths.' This is a specific application of the broader maxim 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative).
The full Arabic: قُدِّمَ عُرْفُ الاسْتِعْمَالِ خُصُوصًا فِي الْأَيْمَانِ.
2
Understand the Rule's Purpose
Oaths are interpreted according to what the swearer likely meant, not the dictionary or Quranic sense. This prevents accidental violations based on technicalities. The swearer's intention is inferred from common usage.
The maxim protects people from unintended consequences of their words.
3
Apply to Main Examples
For each example in the text (bed, meat, animal, roof), identify the Quranic usage vs common custom. E.g., Allah calls the sun a 'lamp' but people don't call sunlight 'lamp light'. Therefore, swearing not to use a lamp is not broken by using sunlight.
Fish is called 'meat' in the Quran, but custom does not consider it meat for oaths.
4
Identify the Exceptions (Istithna'at)
Four cases where sharia definitions override custom: (1) swearing not to pray – funeral prayer excluded; (2) swearing not to fast – only the shar’i fast after dawn counts; (3) swearing not to marry a specific woman – contract breaks oath; (4) 'seeing the crescent' means knowledge, not literal sight. For each, note the reasoning and evidence.
The exceptions are limited and explicitly stated.
5
Synthesize and Extend
Create a mental map: Custom applies to everyday words like meat, bed, animal, roof. Sharia applies to specific religious terms like prayer, fasting, marriage (in context of proposal), and revelational knowledge. Also note the two extra sub-rules: carrion and anal intercourse are not covered by oaths on 'meat' and 'intercourse' respectively.
The final example about water changed by another substance: consider the predominant element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعارض العُرف مع الشرع :
·
فإذا تعارضا قدم عُرْف الاستعمال خصوصا في الأيمان، فإذا حلف لا يجلس على الفراش أو على البساط أو لا يستضيء بالسراج لم يحنث بجلوسه على الأرض ولا بالاستضاءة بالشمس وإن سماها الله تعالى فراشًا وبساطا وسمى الشمس سراجا.
ولو حلف لا يأكل لحما لم يحنث بأكل لحم السمك وإن سماه الله تعالى لحما في القرآن. ولو حلف لا يركب دابَّة، فركب كافرًا لم يحنث وإن سماه الله تعالى دابة.
ولو حلف لا يجلس تحت سقف فجلس تحت السماء، لم يحنث وإن سماها الله تعالى سقفًا . ذلك إلا في مسائل فيقدم الشرع على العُرْف:
الأولى : لو حلف لا يصلي لم يحنث بصلاة الجنازة كما في عامة الكتب .
الثانية : لو حلف لا يصوم لم يحنث بمطلق الإمساك وإنما يحنث بعد طلوع الفجر بنيته أهله . من بصوم ساعة
الثالثة : لو حلف لا ينكح فلانة حنث بالعقد لأنه النكاح الشائع شرعًا لا بالوطء كما في كشف الأسرار، بخلاف لا ينكح زوجته فإنه للوطء.
الرابعة لو قال لها : إن رأيت الهلال فأنت طالق فعلمت به من غير رؤية ينبغي أن يقع لكون الشارع استعمل الرؤية فيه بمعنى العلم في قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «صوموا لرؤيته وأفطروا لرؤيته» (۱) فلو كان الشرع يقتضي الخصوص واللفظ يقتضي العموم اعتبرنا خصوص الشرع. قالوا : لو أوصى لأقاربه لا يدخل الوارث اعتبارًا لخصوص الشرع، ولا يدخل الوالدان والولد للعُزف وهنا فرعان مخرجان لم أرهما الآن صريحًا؛ أحدهما حلف لا يأكل لحما لم يحنث بأكل الميتة. الثاني: حلف لا يطأ لم يحنث بالوطء في الدبر، وأما لو حلف لا يشرب ماء فشرب ماء تغيَّر بغيره فالعبرة للغالب كما صرحوا به في الرضاع.
Section on Conflict of Custom with Sharia:
So if they conflict, the custom of usage is given precedence, especially in oaths. If someone swears not to sit on a bed or a carpet, or not to use a lamp for light, he does not break his oath by sitting on the ground or by using sunlight, even though Allah calls them 'bed' and 'carpet' and calls the sun a 'lamp'.
And if he swears not to eat meat, he does not break his oath by eating fish meat, even though Allah calls it 'meat' in the Quran. And if he swears not to ride an animal, and he rides an infidel, he does not break his oath, even though Allah calls him an 'animal'.
And if he swears not to sit under a roof, and he sits under the sky, he does not break his oath, even though Allah calls it a 'roof'.
That is except in issues where sharia prevails over custom:
First: If he swears not to pray, he does not break his oath with the funeral prayer, as in most books.
Second: If he swears not to fast, he does not break his oath with mere abstention, rather he breaks only after dawn with the intention of fasting for an hour.
Third: If he swears not to marry a woman, he breaks his oath by the marriage contract because it is the prevalent marriage in sharia, not by intercourse, contrary to if he swears not to marry his wife, then it refers to intercourse.
Fourth: If he says to her: 'If you see the crescent, you are divorced', and she knows it without seeing, it should occur because the Lawgiver used 'seeing' to mean knowledge in his saying (peace be upon him): 'Fast by its sighting and break your fast by its sighting.' So if sharia dictates specificity and the wording dictates generality, we consider the specificity of sharia. They said: If he bequeaths to his relatives, the heir is not included, considering the specificity of sharia, and the parents and child are not included due to custom. And here are two derived sub-issues that I have not seen explicitly now: One is that if he swears not to eat meat, he does not break his oath by eating carrion. Second: If he swears not to have intercourse, he does not break his oath by anal intercourse. And regarding if he swears not to drink water, then drinks water that has changed by something else, the consideration is for the predominant, as they stated in the chapter on suckling.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This is a classic exposition of a major legal principle: in oaths, custom (urf) takes precedence over the literal or shar'i meaning of words, except where sharia explicitly dictates otherwise. The author first gives examples where Allah's own usage in the Quran does not override common custom: sitting on the ground does not break an oath about sitting on a 'bed'; eating fish does not break an oath about 'meat'; riding an infidel does not break an oath about riding an 'animal'; sitting under the sky does not break an oath about sitting under a 'roof'. These show that oaths are interpreted according to what people commonly understand, not the divine usage. Then, four exceptions are listed where the shar'i meaning prevails: (1) 'prayer' in an oath does not include funeral prayer because it is not the common prayer; (2) 'fasting' requires the specific shar'i fast after dawn, not mere abstention; (3) 'marriage' in an oath about a specific woman means the contract, not intercourse, but for one's wife, it means intercourse; (4) 'seeing' the crescent in a divorce condition means knowledge of its sighting, based on the hadith. The passage concludes with two derived sub-rules: eating carrion does not break an oath on 'meat', and anal intercourse does not break an oath on 'intercourse'. Finally, in drinking water, if it has changed, the dominant element is considered. This section illustrates the qawida: 'custom is authoritative' (العادة محكمة) with specific exceptions.

Key Terms
عرف الاستعمال (urf al-isti'māl): Customary usage of words among people
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath, swearing
خصوص الشرع (khuṣūṣ al-shar'): Specific meaning given by sharia to a term
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception to the general rule
دابة (dābba): Animal that walks on earth, used in Quran for disbelievers metaphorically

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone swears not to sit under a 'sakf' (roof) and then sits under the sky (samā'), what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because the sky is called a roof in the Quran.
B) He does not break his oath because custom does not call the sky a roof.
C) He breaks his oath if he intended the literal meaning.
D) The oath is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when an oath uses ordinary words, the customary meaning controls, except when sharia has assigned a specific technical meaning to a term for that context.

The Maxim: What is Known by Custom is Like a Stipulated Condition
Introduces a legal maxim equating consistent custom to an explicit condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shar'an', explain its meaning, and apply it to a contractual scenario in ijarah.

Core Idea
Consistent custom has the same legal force as an explicit condition in contracts.

In Hanafi law, custom ('urf) plays a vital role in shaping contractual obligations. This lesson examines a foundational maxim from the text Ijarah al-Zuhayriyyah, which declares that what is widely recognized by custom is treated as if it were expressly stipulated. This principle has far-reaching implications for muamalat, especially in service contracts where customary terms fill in what is unsaid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim
Locate the key phrase 'والمعروف عُرفًا كالمشروط شرعًا' from the source. This is the core legal principle of the lesson.
2
Understand 'Custom as Condition'
Break down the term: 'ma'ruf' = known/recognized; 'urfan' = customarily; 'ka al-mashrut shar'an' = like a condition stipulated by Sharia. The maxim equates a consistent, widespread custom to a written or verbal condition.
Applies in transactions (muamalat), not in matters of worship (ibadat).
3
Apply to the Tailor Example
The source begins an example: giving cloth to a tailor. Customary sewing practices—such as type of stitch, thread color, or completion time—become binding as if they were explicitly stipulated. Complete the scenario in your mind: the tailor must follow local custom.
The text cuts off, but the principle is clear.
4
Connect to Mufti Practice
As a mufti, when issuing fatwas on contracts, you must ascertain the prevailing custom. If a term is widely assumed and consistently practiced, it is enforceable even if not written. This is a key tool for resolving modern disputes.
This maxim is cited in many Hanafi fatwa collections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : العادة المطَّردة هل تنزل منزلة الشرط؟ قال في إجارة الظهيرية : والمعروف عُرفًا كالمشروط شرعًا. (انتهى) وقالوا في الإجارات لو دفع ثوبًا إلى خياط ليخيطه له أو
The Third Investigation: Does consistent custom take the place of a condition? It is said in Ijarah al-Zuhayriyyah: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by Shariah.' (End) And they said in [the chapter of] ijarahs: If he gave a cloth to a tailor to sew it for him, or...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is a short but important passage introducing the third investigation (mabahith) on the status of consistent custom (al-‘adah al-muttaridah). It asks whether such custom has the same legal effect as an explicit condition. The answer is cited from the text Ijarah al-Zuhayriyyah: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by Shariah.' This means that when a practice is so widespread and consistent that it is taken for granted, it is legally equivalent to an explicit condition in contracts. The passage begins to give an example from the chapter of ijarah: if someone gives cloth to a tailor for sewing, the customary terms (like type of stitch, timeframe) are binding as if they were stipulated. The text is cut off, but the principle is clear: custom functions as an implied condition in transactions.

Key Terms
عادة مطردة (adah muttaridah): A consistent, prevailing custom that is widespread and regularly followed.
الظهيرية (al-Zuhayriyyah): A Hanafi text on ijarah, likely by a scholar named al-Zuhayri.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what legal weight does a consistent custom (عادة مطردة) have?
A) It is completely ignored unless explicitly stated.
B) It is considered like an explicitly stated condition.
C) It only applies in matters of worship.
D) It is binding only if the parties agree in writing.

Key Takeaway
Consistent custom in transactions carries the same legal weight as an explicit condition.

Concurrent Custom Only in Interpretation
Establishes that only concurrent custom can interpret texts, not subsequent custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida that only concurrent custom interprets words, distinguishing between transactions and conditional statements.

Core Idea
Only the custom existing at the time of utterance can interpret words; later custom has no effect.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir establishes a key distinction: the custom that existed when the words were uttered governs interpretation, not later changes. For muftis, this prevents retroactive reinterpretation of contracts and oaths.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Memorize the qawida in Arabic: العُرْف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر. Understand its meaning: only the custom concurrent with the speech is considered, not subsequent custom.
2
Distinguish the two domains
Custom is considered in transactions (mu'amalat) because they rely on implied terms. In conditional statements (ta'liq) like oaths, later custom does not specify; they remain on literal generality.
3
Analyze the al-Mabsut example
A man forced by his wife to swear says 'every jariyah I buy is free,' intending ships. His intention is valid because concurrent Quranic custom used 'jariyah' for ships. No emancipation occurs.
The wife's oppression is context, but the key is the concurrent custom.
4
Apply to a new case
Suppose someone vows not to eat 'fakiha' (fruit). At his time, it meant only dates. Later, mangoes are introduced and called 'fakiha'. Does the vow include mangoes? No, only the concurrent custom at the time of the vow matters.
This illustrates the rule's practical impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: العُرْف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر؛ ولذا قالوا لا عبرة بالعُرف الطارىء فلذا اعتبر العُرف في المعاملات ولم يعتبر في التعليق فيبقى على عمومه ولا يخصّصه العُرْف. وفي آخر المبسوط: إذا أراد الرجل أن يغيب فحلفته امرأته فقال كل جارية اشتريتها فهي حرَّة، وهو يعني كل سفينة جارية، عمل بنيته ولا يقع عليه العتق قال الله تعالى: وله الجواري المُنشَآت في البحر كالأعلام [الرحمن: ٢٤] والمراد السفن، فإذا نوى ذلك عملت نيته لأنها ظالمة في هذا
The fourth section: The custom by which words are to be interpreted is only the concurrent preceding custom, not the subsequent one. Hence they said: no consideration is given to a later-emerging custom. Therefore custom is considered in transactions (mu'amalat) but not in conditional statements (ta'liq); [the latter] remains on its generality and is not specified by custom. In the end of al-Mabsut: if a man intended to travel and his wife made him swear, and he said 'every jariyah I buy is free,' intending by that every ship (safinah jariyah), his intention is acted upon and no emancipation occurs. Allah said: 'And His are the ships raised up in the sea like mountains' (Quran 55:24) meaning ships. So if he intended that, his intention is given effect because she was being oppressive in this...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines a critical rule for applying custom in legal interpretation: only custom that existed at the time of the speech or contract ('urf muqarin sabaq) is considered. A later-emerging custom ('urf tari') cannot reinterpret past words. Consequently, custom is applied in transactions (mu'amalat) where it can imply terms, but not in conditional statements (ta'liq) like oaths or divorces, which remain on their literal generality and are not narrowed by later custom. An example from Al-Mabsut illustrates: a man, forced by his wife to swear, says 'Every jariyah I buy is free,' but he meant by 'jariyah' a ship (safinah jariyah), as in the Quranic usage. Since his intention was for ships, his oath does not emancipate any slave. The custom of his time and context (the wife's oppression) allows considering his intent, but the key is the concurrent meaning at the time of utterance, not later usage.

Key Terms
العرف المقارن (al-'urf al-muqarin): Custom that exists concurrently with the speech or act, used to interpret intent
العرف الطارىء (al-'urf al-tari'): Custom that emerges after the speech or act; not considered in interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements is true about custom in Hanafi legal interpretation?
A) Custom always interprets words regardless of timing
B) Only concurrent custom is considered
C) Later custom can specify general terms
D) Custom is not considered in transactions

Key Takeaway
Only the custom that existed at the time of the utterance or contract can be used to interpret its meaning; subsequent custom has no bearing.

intermediate

Custom in Oaths and Conditional Statements
Explaining that only preceding concurrent custom governs wording, not later custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim that only preceding concurrent custom (al-muqarin al-sabiq) governs the interpretation of oaths and conditional statements, and distinguish between mu'amalat and ta'liq in custom-based interpretation.

Core Idea
Only the custom at the time of the oath governs its meaning; later custom and literal meaning can be overridden by intention in cases of oppression.

This lesson introduces a crucial Hanafi maxim that limits the role of custom in interpreting oaths and conditional statements. Unlike transactions (mu'amalat), where custom can clarify intent, in oaths the speaker's intention at the moment of utterance is paramount, especially when the oath was unjustly extracted. The text from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir and al-Mabsut provides the framework and vivid examples.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Read the maxim in Arabic: العُرْف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر. Understand that 'preceding concurrent' means the custom present at the time of utterance, not later emerging custom.
The dabt (sub-maxim) is: لا عبرة بالعرف الطارئ (no consideration to emergent custom).
2
Identify the two domains
Recognise that custom is considered in mu'amalat (transactions) but not in ta'liq (conditional statements like oaths and conditional divorce). The reason: ta'liq is fixed at the moment of utterance, while mu'amalat are contemporary with custom.
This distinction is critical for applying the maxim correctly.
3
Analyze the first furu'
Examine the case of the man swearing 'Every jariyah I buy is free' meaning ships. The text accepts his intention because (1) the word jariyah can mean ship as per Quran 55:24, and (2) the wife was oppressive in demanding the oath, so the oppressed's intention is considered.
The Quranic evidence supports that jariyah has a legitimate secondary meaning.
4
Analyze the second furu'
Examine the conditional divorce case where the man intends 'on your neck' (i.e., against her). His intention is acted upon because he intended the reality of his words (haqiqat kalamihi).
This reinforces that intention overrides apparent meaning in ta'liq.
5
Reflect on the exception
Understand that oppression (zulm) triggers the consideration of intention. Without oppression, the general maxim still applies: custom is not used to restrict ta'liq. The related qawida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' supports this.
The exception does not weaken the maxim but highlights its nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع :
العُرْف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر؛ ولذا قالوا لا عبرة بالعُزف الطارىء فلذا اعتبر العُزف في المعاملات ولم يعتبر في التعليق فيبقى على عمومه ولا يخصصه العُرْف. وفي آخر المبسوط : إذا أراد الرجل أن يغيب فحلفته امرأته فقال كل جارية اشتريتها فهي حرَّة، وهو يعني كل سفينة جارية، عمل بنيته ولا يقع عليه العتق قال الله تعالى : وله الجواري المُنشَآت في البحر كالأعلام [الرحمن: ٢٤] والمراد السفن، فإذا نوى ذلك عملت نيته لأنها ظالمة في هذا الاستحلاف ونيَّة المظلوم فيما يحلف عليه ،معتبرة، وإن حلفته بطلاق كل امرأة أتزوجها عليك، فليقل كل امرأة أتزوجها عليك فهي طالق وهو ينوي بذلك كل امرأة أتزوجها على رقبتك فيعمل بنيته لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه . (اهـ).
The fourth section: The custom upon which words are construed is only the preceding concurrent custom, not the later; hence they said: no consideration is given to emergent custom. Therefore custom is considered in transactions but not in conditional statements (ta'liq), which remain on their generality and custom does not specify them. In the end of al-Mabsut: If a man intends to travel and his wife makes him swear, and he says "Every slave-girl I buy is free," meaning every sailing ship (safina), he acts on his intention and no emancipation occurs. Allah said: "And His are the ships that sail in the sea like landmarks" [al-Rahman 24] meaning ships. So if he intends that, his intention is acted upon because she is oppressive in this swearing, and the intention of the oppressed in what he swears about is considered. And if she makes him swear by divorce that every woman he marries against her, then he should say: "Every woman I marry against you is divorced" intending by that every woman he marries on her neck (i.e., against her), then his intention is acted upon because he intended the reality of his words. (End).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a legal maxim (qawida) concerning the application of custom to words. It opens the fourth section (mabahith) stating that the custom used to interpret words is only the preceding concurrent custom (al-muqarin al-sabiq), not later custom. Therefore, emergent custom (al-'urf al-tari') is disregarded. Custom is considered in transactions (mu'amalat) because they are contemporary, but it is not considered in conditional statements (ta'liq), such as oaths and conditional divorce. Conditional statements remain on their general meaning and are not restricted by later custom. The text then provides two examples from al-Mabsut: (1) A woman makes her husband swear before travel, and he says, 'Every jariyah I buy is free,' intending by 'jariyah' a ship (safina). Despite the common meaning of 'jariyah' being a slave-girl, the husband's intention is accepted because he intended the literal meaning (ships) as supported by Quran 55:24, and because the wife was oppressive in demanding the oath. The intention of the oppressed is considered. (2) If she makes him swear by divorce that every woman he marries against her is divorced, and he intends 'every woman I marry on your neck' (i.e., against you), his intention is acted upon because he intended the reality of his words. These examples illustrate that in oaths, the speaker's intention may override the default meaning, and custom is not used to interpret the oath because custom can change, whereas the oath is fixed at the time of utterance.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement that suspends a legal consequence on the occurrence of a future event, such as oaths and conditional divorce
المقارن السابق (al-muqarin al-sabiq): The custom that coexists with the utterance, not a later custom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the man's intention accepted when he swears 'Every jariyah I buy is free' meaning ships?
A) Because the word 'jariyah' literally means ship in Arabic
B) Because his wife was oppressive in demanding the oath, and the oppressed's intention is considered
C) Because custom at that time defined 'jariyah' as ship
D) Because he added a verbal condition confirming his intention

Key Takeaway
Only the custom concurrent with an oath matters; later custom and literal meaning can be overridden by the speaker's intention, especially when the oath was coerced.

Custom in Oaths vs. Contracts: The Hanafi Distinction
Distinguishing custom's role in oaths/acknowledgments versus contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-urf fi al-uqud duna al-ayman wa al-iqrar' to distinguish between cases where custom governs and where it does not.

Core Idea
Custom governs contracts but not oaths or acknowledgments: past events override present custom.

In Hanafi law, custom (urf) is a recognized source for interpreting contracts, but its scope is limited. This lesson examines a key qawida: custom applies to contracts but not to oaths and acknowledgments. Understanding this principle is crucial for a mufti when analyzing cases where parties invoke custom to interpret or vary their statements.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the rule: custom governs contracts but not oaths and acknowledgments. Note the Arabic formulation and its rank (wusta).
The qawida is a middle principle (wusta) that regulates several fiqh chapters.
2
Identify its scope
Determine which chapters are affected: oaths (ayman), acknowledgments (iqrar), contracts (uqud), and endowments (waqf).
Custom here means the prevalent practice of people, not mere usage.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the maxim to the cases given: oaths depend on intent; acknowledgments are past-referring; contracts are present and thus restricted by custom.
Consider the example of stipends in schools: custom after endowment does not affect prior endowments.
4
Identify the istithna
Note exceptions: when an oath is tied to a specific ruler, removal of the ruler voids the oath because it is conditioned on that person, not the office.
This exception is governed by the qawida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are by their intentions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستحلاف ونيَّة المظلوم فيما يحلف عليه ،معتبرة، وإن حلفته بطلاق كل امرأة أتزوجها عليك، فليقل كل امرأة أتزوجها عليك فهي طالق وهو ينوي بذلك كل امرأة أتزوجها على رقبتك فيعمل بنيته لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه . (اهـ).
فلا
وأما الإقرار فهو إخبار عن وجوب ،سابق وربما يقدّم الوجوب على العُرْف الغالب وكذا لو أقرَّ بدراهم ثم فسَّرها أنها زيوف (۱) أو تبهرجة (٢) يصدق إن وصل. وإن أقر بألف من ثمن متاع أو قرض لم يصدق عند الإمام إذا قال هي زيوف وصل أو فصل وصدقاه إن وصل وإن أقرَّ بألف غصبًا أو وديعة ثم قال زيوف صدق هي مطلقا. وكذا الدعوى لا تنزل على العادة لأن الدعوى والإقرار إخبار بما تقدم يقيده العُزف المتأخر بخلاف العقد فإنه باشره للحال فقيَّده العرف. قال في البزازية من الدعوى معزيا إلى اللامشي إذا كانت النقود في البلد مختلفة أحدها أروج لا تصح الدعوى ما لم يبين وكذا لو أقرَّ بعشرة دنانير حمر وفي البلد نقود مختلفة حمر تصح بلا بیان، بخلاف البيع فإنه ينصرف إلى الأروج . (انتهى) .
لا
وقد أوسعنا الكلام على ذلك في شرح الكنز من أول البيع. ويمكن أن تخرج عليها مسألتان :إحداهما مسألة البطالة في المدارس فإذا استمر عرف بها في أشهر مخصوصة حمل عليها ما وقف بعدها لا ما وقف قبلها الثانية : إذا شرط الواقف النظر للحاكم وكان الحاكم إذ ذاك شافعيًا ثم صار الآن حنفيا لا قاضي غيره إلا نيابة . هل يكون النظر له لأنه الحاكم أولاً لأنه متأخر فلا يحمل المتقدم عليه؟ فمقتضى القاعدة الثاني. ولكن قالوا في الأيمان: لو حلفه والي بلدة ليعلمه بكل داعر دخل البلدة بطلت اليمين بعزل الوالي فلا يحنث إذا لم يعلم الوالي الثاني.
ولم أرَ الآن حكم ما إذا حلف متى رأى منكرًا رفعه إلى القاضي؛ هل تعين القاضي حالة اليمين؟ ومن هذا النوع لو وقف بلدا على الحرم الشريف وشرط النظر للقاضي هل ينصرف إلى قاضي الحرم أو قاضي البلدة الموقوفة أو قاضي بلد الواقف؟ ينبغي أن يستخرج من مسألة ما لو كان اليتيم في بلد وماله في بلد آخر فهل النظر عليه لقاضي بلد اليتيم أو لقاضي بلد ماله؟ صرَّحوا بالأول فينبغي أن يكون النظر لقاضي الحرم. ويمكن أن يقال إن الأرجح كون النظر لقاضي البلد الموقوفة لأنه أعرف بمصالحها فالظاهر أن الواقف قصده وبه تحصل المصلحة. وقد اختلفوا فيما إذا كان العقار لا في ولاية القاضي وتنازعا فيه عند قاض آخر، فمنهم من لم يصحح قضاءه، ومنهم من نظر إلى التداعي والترافع واختلف التصحيح في هذه المسألة.
The oath and the intention of the wronged party regarding what he is sworn upon are considered. If one swears him by divorce saying 'every woman I marry upon you is divorced,' let him say 'every woman I marry upon you is divorced' intending thereby every woman he marries upon his neck, and his intention is acted upon because he intended the reality of his speech. (End). So no. As for the acknowledgment, it is an information of a prior obligation, and sometimes the obligation takes precedence over the prevailing custom. Similarly, if one acknowledges dirhams and then interprets them as counterfeit or debased, he is believed if he specified at the time. If he acknowledges a thousand as the price of goods or a loan, he is not believed according to Imam Abu Hanifa if he says they were counterfeit, whether specified at the time or later; but the two companions believe him if specified at the time. If he acknowledges a thousand as usurped or a deposit, then says they were counterfeit, he is believed absolutely. Likewise, a claim is not interpreted according to custom because the claim and acknowledgment inform of a past event, which is restricted by later custom, unlike the contract which is executed immediately, so it is restricted by custom. It is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah from al-Lamishi: if coins in the country are different, one being more prevalent, the claim is not valid unless specified. Similarly, if one acknowledges ten red dinars and in the country there are different red coins, it is valid without specification, contrary to sale, which refers to the most prevalent. (End). No. We have expanded the discussion on this in Sharh al-Kanz from the beginning of the book of sale. Two issues can be derived from this: first, the issue of stipends in schools: if a custom persists in specific months, it applies to what was endowed after it, not what was endowed before. Second: if the endower stipulates the oversight of the judge and the judge at that time was Shafii, but now he is Hanafi and there is no other judge except by deputyship, does the oversight belong to him because he is the judge, or because later custom does not carry the earlier? The implication of the principle is the second. But they said regarding oaths: if the ruler of a town swore him to inform him of every mischief-maker entering the town, the oath becomes void upon the ruler's dismissal, and he does not break the oath if he does not inform the second ruler. I have not seen now the ruling if one swears that whenever he sees an objectionable thing he will report it to the judge: does the judge become specified at the time of the oath? Also of this type: if one endows a town on the noble sanctuary and stipulates oversight for the judge, does it refer to the judge of the sanctuary, the judge of the endowed town, or the judge of the endower's town? It should be derived from the issue where the orphan is in one town and his wealth in another: does oversight belong to the judge of the orphan's town or the judge of the wealth's town? They declared the first, so it should be the oversight of the judge of the sanctuary. It can be said that the preponderant is that the oversight belongs to the judge of the endowed town because he is more aware of its interests, so apparently the endower intended that and benefit is achieved thereby. They differed regarding when the property is not in the judge's jurisdiction and they dispute before another judge: some did not validate his judgment, some considered the litigation and appeal, and the validation differed in this issue.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a qawida-based discussion on the role of customary practice (urf) in different legal contexts, distinguishing between oaths, acknowledgments, and contracts. The passage begins by establishing that in oaths, the intention of the person swearing is given precedence over customary usage, even if the wording seems general. For example, if someone makes an oath contingent on marrying a woman upon a person, and the person swears intending a specific meaning, that meaning is acted upon. This is because oaths are tied to the intent of the oath-taker. In acknowledgments (iqrar), the principle is that they inform of a past obligation, and thus custom is not automatically applied because the event is prior. However, there are nuances: if one acknowledges dirhams and later claims they were counterfeit, he is believed if he specified at the time (wusul). If he acknowledges a sum as price of goods or loan, Imam Abu Hanifa does not accept a later claim of counterfeit, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) accept it if specified at the time. For claims of usurpation or deposit, he is believed absolutely due to the nature of the claim. The reason for the distinction is that contracts are executed immediately and thus are restricted by prevailing custom, but claims and acknowledgments relate to past events and custom at the time of the claim cannot alter past reality. The passage then provides two derived issues: first, if a custom of stipends in schools exists for specific months, that custom applies to endowments made after the custom but not before. Second, if an endowment stipulates the oversight of a judge, and the judge changes madhhab, the later judge does not inherit oversight because the custom at the time of endowment is considered. However, in oaths, if the ruler who administered the oath is removed, the oath becomes void because it was tied to that specific person. The passage ends with a discussion on which judge has oversight in certain scenarios, emphasizing that the endower's intent and the benefit of the beneficiaries are considered. The overall rule is that custom governs contracts but not oaths or acknowledgments where intent or past reality is primary.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice that governs the interpretation of contracts but not past events like oaths and acknowledgments.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or obligation, treated as informing of a past event, not subject to later customary interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is custom not applied to acknowledgments (iqrar) in the same way as contracts?
A) Because acknowledgments are a form of worship.
B) Because acknowledgments are informative of a past obligation, and custom at the time of acknowledgment cannot change past reality.
C) Because acknowledgments require explicit verbal specification.
D) Because custom is only considered in disputes.

Key Takeaway
Custom governs contracts but not oaths or acknowledgments because the latter are tied to intention or past events, not present transactions.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
General vs. Specific Custom in Legal Rulings
Determining whether general or specific custom establishes legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-hukm al-amm la yuthbit bi al-urf al-khaas' to distinguish between valid and invalid customs in Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
A general ruling is not established by specific custom; only widespread custom grounds a legal rule.

When you encounter a local practice or a custom limited to an elite group, can you rely on it to issue a fatwa? The Hanafi school answers with caution: general custom (urf aam) is the basis for legal rulings, while specific custom (urf khaas) is insufficient. This lesson unpacks the key maxim and its implications through concrete examples from classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the core principle: 'A general ruling is not established by specific custom.' Understand its purpose: to maintain uniformity and prevent fragmentation of the law.
The maxim is attributed to Imam al-Bukhari.
2
Analyze the furuat (branches)
Examine each case in the passage – the borrower hiring the lender, non-standard weights, weaving contracts – and see how the maxim is applied. Note why the specific custom fails to establish a valid contract.
In each furu, the custom was limited to a locality or elite group.
3
Identify the istithna' (exception)
Look for cases where specific custom was accepted, such as the Balkh weavers or bay' al-wafa. Understand the condition: the custom became widespread enough to avoid hardship.
The exception is governed by the maxim 'al-'aadah muhakkamah'.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
Compare the positions: the preferred Hanafi position rejects specific custom as a basis for general rulings, but some scholars allowed it in practice. Determine when a mufti may follow the exception.
The author sides with the majority, but acknowledges the practical fatwa of some scholars.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Given a real or hypothetical situation (e.g., a modern financial practice), test whether the custom meets the criteria of 'urf aam or remains 'urf khaas. Then decide the ruling.
Use the structure: case → qawida → application → ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

هل يعتبر في بناء الأحكام العُرْف العام أو مطلق العُرْف ولو كان خاصا؟ المذهب الأول. قال في البزازية معزيّا إلى الإمام البخاري الذي ختم به الفقه : الحكم العام لا يثبت بالعُرْف الخاص وقيل يثبت. (انتهى).
ويتفرع على ذلك لو استقرض ألفًا واستأجر المقرض لحفظ مرآة أو ملعقة كل شهر بعشرة وقيمتها لا تزيد على الأخر ففيها ثلاثة أقوال صحة الإجارة بلا كراهة اعتبارًا لعُزف خواص بخارى والصحة مع الكراهة للاختلاف، والفساد لأن صحة الإجارة بالتعارف العام ولم يوجد وقد أفتى الأكابر بفسادها. وفي القنية من باب استئجار المستقرض المقرض : التعارف الذي تثبت به الأحكام لا يثبت بتعارف أهل بلدة واحدة عند البعض. وعند البعض إن كان يثبت ولكن أحدثه بعض أهل بخارى فلم يكن متعارفًا مطلقا كيف وأن هذا الشيء لم يعرفه عامتهم بل تعارفه خواصهم فلا يثبت التعارف بهذا القدر، قال رضي الله عنه : وهو الصواب. (انتهى).
وذكر فيها من كتاب الكراهية قبيل التحرّي ؛ لو تواضع أهل بلدة على زيادة في سنجاتهم التي توزن بها الدراهم والإبريسم (۱) على مخالفة سائر البلدان ليس لهم ذلك . (انتهى). وفي إجارة البزازية في إجارة الأصل؛ استأجره ليحمل طعامه بقفيز منه فالإجارة فاسدة ويجب أجر المثل لا يتجاور به المسمى، وكذا إذا دفع إلى حائك غزلاً على أن ينسجه بالثلث. ومشايخ بلخ وخوارزم أفتوا بجواز إجارة الحائك للعزف وبه أفتى أبو علي النسفي أيضًا الفتوى على جواب الكتاب لا الطحان لأنه منصوص عليه فيلزم إبطال النص. (انتهى).
أنه وفيها من البيع الفاسد في الكلام على بيع الوفاء في القول السادس من صحيح قالوا لحاجة الناس إليه فرارًا من الربا؛ فأهل بلخ اعتادوا الدين والإجارة وهي
Caution: Is it considered in building rulings general custom or absolute custom even if specific? The first position. It is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah attributed to Imam al-Bukhari who sealed fiqh: 'A general ruling is not established by specific custom, and it is said it is established.' (End). Branching from this: if one borrows one thousand and hires the lender to preserve a mirror or spoon each month for ten, and its value does not exceed the other, then there are three opinions: validity of the lease without dislike, considering the custom of the elite of Bukhara; validity with dislike due to disagreement; and invalidity because the validity of the lease is by general custom and it is not found. The leading scholars have issued a fatwa of its invalidity. In al-Qunyah from the chapter of hiring the lender by the borrower: 'The customary practice by which rulings are established is not established by the custom of the people of one town according to some. According to some, it is established, but it was innovated by some of the people of Bukhara, so it was not customary absolutely, especially since this thing was not known to their commoners but rather their elite custom, so custom is not established by this extent.' Rida Allah said: That is the correct view. (End). It is mentioned in it from the book of dislike before the section on taharri: if the people of a town agree on an increase in their weights by which dirhams and silk are weighed, contrary to other towns, they do not have that right. (End). In the lease section of al-Bazzaziyyah on the lease of capital: he hired him to carry his food for a qafiz of it, so the lease is invalid and the comparable wage is obligatory, not exceeding the named wage. Likewise, if he gave thread to a weaver to weave it for a third. The scholars of Balkh and Khwarazm issued a fatwa permitting the lease of the weaver for custom, and Abu Ali al-Nasafi also gave a fatwa: the fatwa is upon the answer of the book, not the miller, because it is textually provided, so it necessitates invalidating the text. (End). And in it from the invalid sale in the discussion on bay' al-wafa in the sixth saying of Sahih, they said: due to people's need for it as an escape from riba; the people of Balkh were accustomed to debt and lease, and it is
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental question in Hanafi legal theory: which type of custom (urf) is recognized for establishing general legal rulings? The preferred view (madhhab) is that only general custom (urf aam) is considered; specific custom (urf khaas) is not sufficient. This is attributed to Imam Bukhari. The passage then provides several applications. First, an example where a person borrows money and hires the lender to preserve an item for a nominal fee: since this practice was known only among the elite of Bukhara (urf khaas) and not the general public, many jurists (akabir) declared such a contract invalid, because it lacks general custom. Second, it is stated that agreement among the people of one town on a non-standard weight measure is not permissible, as it violates general custom. Third, hiring a weaver with a share of the cloth is invalid in the original lease ruling, but scholars from Balkh and Khwarazm permitted it based on their local custom; however, this is a departure from the clear text (nass) and is criticized. Finally, the passage mentions bay' al-wafa (a sale with repurchase) as a device to avoid riba, noting that the people of Balkh were accustomed to it. The overall message is that jurists must be cautious: custom that is limited to a specific locality or elite group cannot override general rulings established by texts or general custom.

Key Terms
العرف العام (al-urf al-aam): Custom that is widespread among the majority of people in its time and place, used to establish general rulings.
العرف الخاص (al-urf al-khaas): Custom limited to a specific locality, group, or elite, not sufficient for general rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a contract based on a custom practiced only by the elite of a particular town?
A) It is valid because custom is always accepted.
B) It is invalid because general custom has not been established.
C) It is valid with dislike because of difference of opinion.
D) It is valid only if approved by the ruler.

Key Takeaway
Only general custom ('urf aam) can establish a general ruling; specific custom ('urf khaas) is insufficient.

The Six Major Legal Maxims of Hanafi Fiqh
Enumeration of six foundational maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state all six major Hanafi legal maxims in Arabic and explain the core meaning of each.

Core Idea
Six universal maxims govern all fiqh: intention, purpose, certainty, hardship, harm, and custom.

This passage presents the six foundational legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliyya al-sitt) that are the bedrock of Hanafi jurisprudence. These maxims are not independent sources but distillations of rulings from the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma. A mufti relies on them to navigate new cases with consistency. Before we dive into the many specific rules that follow, master these six – they are the keys to the entire madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list and its nature
Recognize that this passage simply enumerates the six universal maxims. They are called 'kubra' (major) because they apply across all chapters of fiqh.
These are not the only maxims, but the most comprehensive.
2
Memorize each maxim in Arabic and English
Learn the Arabic phrases and their translations. These are frequently quoted in fatwa literature.
Use the exact wording from the source.
3
Understand the core meaning of each maxim
For each maxim, grasp the basic legal principle it conveys. For example, 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' preserves established rulings until definitive evidence overturns them.
Each maxim has rich applications; focus on the general idea now.
4
Relate them to their sources
Though not stated here, these maxims are derived from revealed texts – e.g., the first is from the hadith 'Innama al-a'mal bi al-niyat'.
This connects them to the foundational sources of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمت القواعد الكلية وهي :ست الأولى لا ثواب إلا بالنية الثانية : الأمور : بمقاصدها الثالثة : اليقين لا يزول بالشك. الرابعة : المشقّة تجلب التيسير الخامسة : الضرر يُزال. السادسة : العادة محكمة.
And the universal maxims have been completed, and they are six: the first: there is no reward except through intention. The second: matters are judged by their purposes. The third: certainty is not removed by doubt. The fourth: hardship brings ease. The fifth: harm must be removed. The sixth: custom is authoritative.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a simple listing of the six major legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliyya al-sitt) that form the backbone of Hanafi fiqh. These maxims are universal principles that apply across all chapters of law. They are: (1) Actions are judged by intentions – a maxim from hadith affecting ibadat and contracts. (2) Matters are judged by their purposes – focusing on intent in legal acts. (3) Certainty is not removed by doubt – preserving established rulings unless certainty is overturned. (4) Hardship brings ease – allowing concessions when there is difficulty. (5) Harm must be removed – preventing damage and requiring compensation. (6) Custom is authoritative – giving weight to established practices. The author lists them here as a prelude to discussing more detailed rules in the second type of qawaid.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawaid al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that encompass many specific rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) الأمور بمقاصدها
B) اليقين لا يزول بالشك
C) الاجتهاد لا ينقض بالاجتهاد
D) العادة محكمة

Key Takeaway
Memorize the six major maxims; they are the lenses through which a Hanafi mufti views every new case.
