The Fourteenth Maxim: What is Forbidden to Take is Forbidden to Give
Legal maxim with furuat and istithna'at

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه' to identify permissible exceptions and construct a fatwa on a related case.

Core Idea
Prohibition of taking implies prohibition of giving, except when necessity or saving a life overrides.

This lesson introduces the fourteenth legal maxim, which establishes that anything prohibited to receive is also prohibited to give. The text provides clear examples such as riba, bribery, and fees for prohibited services, then lists exceptions where giving is permissible due to necessity or to preserve life. Understanding this maxim and its istithna'at is essential for a mufti to correctly rule on cases involving giving money or items that are normally haram to take.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Begin by reciting the maxim: 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه'. Understand that it covers all prohibitions in transactions (buyu, ijarah) and criminal law, meaning if receiving something is haram, then giving it is also haram.
The maxim is universal (kulliya) with specific exceptions.
2
List the examples under the general rule
The text lists riba, dower of a prostitute (mahr al-baghi), fortune-teller's fee (hulwan al-kahin), bribery (rashwah), and hire of a wailer and musician. For each, both giving and taking are haram.
These are standard examples from Hanafi fiqh.
3
Identify the exceptions and their reasoning
The exceptions include: bribery under fear for life or property (except to a judge), ransom for a prisoner, giving to avoid slander, and a guardian paying a usurper. The underlying principle is necessity (darura) or preservation of life.
The exception for bribery does NOT apply to judges; for them, both remain haram.
4
Analyze the related maxim
The passage also introduces a parallel maxim: 'ما حرم فعله حرم طلبه' (what is forbidden to do is forbidden to ask for). It mentions two exceptions: administering an oath to a truthful claimant, and demanding jizyah from a dhimmi.
The second exception (jizyah) is noted as not seen transmitted by the author.
5
Apply to a new case
Consider a scenario: a person is threatened by a non-judge official. According to the maxim and its exception, giving a bribe is permissible. For a judge, it remains haram. Practice reasoning through the maxim.
This step prepares for fatwa issuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الرابعة عشرة : ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه كالربا ومهر البغي وحلوان الكاهن والرشوة وأُجرة النائحة والزامر، إلا في مسائل : الرشوة لخوف على نفسه أو ماله ليسوِّي أمره عند سلطان أو أمير، إلا للقاضي فإنه يحرم الأخذ والإعطاء، كما بيَّناه في شرح الكنز من القضاء وفك الأسير وإعطاء شيء لمن يخاف هجوه ولو خاف الوصي أن يستولي غاصب على المال فله أداء شيء ليخلصه كما في الخلاصة. وهل يحلّ دفع الصدقة لمن سأل ومعه قوت يومه؟ تردد الأكمل في شرح المشارق فيه ؛ فمقتضى أصل القاعدة الحرمة إلا أن يقال إن الصدقة هنا هبة كالتصدّق على الغني. تنبيه ويقرب من هذا قاعدة: ما حرم فعله حرم طلبه إلا في مسألتين : الأولى : ادَّعى دعوى صادقة فأنكر الغريم فله تحليفه . الثانية : الجزية يجوز طلبها من الذمّي مع أنه عليه إعطاؤها، لأنه متمكن من إزالة الكفر بالإسلام فإعطاؤه إياها إنما هو لاستمراره على الكفر وهو حرام. والأولى منقولة عندها، ولم أرَ الثانية .
The fourteenth maxim: What is forbidden to take is forbidden to give, such as riba, the dower of a prostitute, the fee of a fortune-teller, bribery, the hire of a wailer and a musician, except in certain issues: Bribery out of fear for one's life or property to settle one's matter with a ruler or prince, except for a judge, for whom taking and giving are both forbidden; as we explained in Sharh al-Kanz in the chapter of judiciary, and the ransom of a prisoner, and giving something to one whose satire one fears. And if the guardian fears that a usurper will seize the property, he may pay something to free it, as in al-Khulasah. And is it permissible to give charity to one who asks while he has enough food for the day? Al-Akmal hesitated in Sharh al-Mashariq; the apparent rule is prohibition, unless it is said that charity here is a gift, like giving to the rich. Note: And near to this is a maxim: What is forbidden to do is forbidden to ask, except in two issues: First: One makes a truthful claim and the debtor denies; he may administer an oath to him. Second: Jizyah may be demanded from a dhimmi although he is obligated to give it, because he is capable of removing disbelief through Islam; so his giving it is only for his persistence in disbelief, which is haram. The first is transmitted with its source; I have not seen the second.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth legal maxim: anything that is haram to receive is also haram to give. Examples include riba (interest), the dower of a prostitute, fees for fortune-telling, bribery, and payments for wailing or music. However, there are exceptions. Bribery is allowed if given out of fear for life or property to a ruler (except a judge, for whom it remains haram). Ransom for a prisoner and payments to avoid slander are also allowed. A guardian may pay a usurper to free property. The author then discusses a related issue: giving charity to someone who already has enough. He notes hesitation but suggests it may be permissible as a gift. Finally, he mentions a related maxim: what is haram to do is haram to ask for, with two exceptions: administering an oath to a denying debtor (which is permissible to ask) and demanding jizyah from a dhimmi (which is permissible to request despite the receiver's sin).

Key Terms
رشوة (rashwah): Bribery: giving money to a judge or official to influence a decision.
حلوان الكاهن (ḥulwān al-kāhin): Fee given to a fortune-teller for telling the future.
ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه (mā ḥurrima akhdhuhu ḥurrima iʿṭāʾuhu): What is forbidden to take is forbidden to give.
الضرورة تبيح المحظورات (al-ḍarūratu tubīḥu al-maḥẓūrāt): Necessity permits the prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourteenth maxim, which of the following is an exception to the rule that what is forbidden to take is forbidden to give?
A) Paying a prostitute her dower
B) Giving a bribe to a judge to save one's life
C) Paying ransom for a prisoner
D) Paying a wailer for mourning

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه' governs transactions, but istithna'at based on necessity and preservation of life are recognized, particularly for bribery to non-judges under duress, ransom, and similar cases.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

The Maxim 'Whoever Hastens Something Before Its Time' with Furu' and Critique
Legal maxim with applications and a scholarly critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, identify its applications, and evaluate whether a given case falls under the maxim or its opposite.

Core Idea
Rushing a benefit before its proper time forfeits the benefit itself.

This lesson examines a major legal maxim that governs many rulings in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim penalizes premature action with deprivation. We will study two classical applications and engage with a scholarly debate about the proper scope of the maxim.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
First, commit the Arabic text of the maxim to memory: مَن استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه. Understand its meaning: whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it.
This is a kulliya (universal) maxim among the five major qawa'id.
2
Study the first furu': the killer's deprivation
The classic application: a murderer who kills his victim to hasten inheritance is deprived of inheriting. This is because he acted prematurely to secure a benefit, so the benefit is taken away.
This is firmly established in Hanafi fara'id.
3
Examine the second furu': the mukatib's delay
Al-Tahawi's case: a mukatib who can pay but delays payment to continue looking at his mistress. The ruling is that this is not permissible. But does this fit the maxim? The author questions whether it's punishment by deprivation or simply prohibition.
Consider the concept of 'hastening' — here the mukatib is delaying, not hastening.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author notes that the mukatib's act is actually delaying, not hastening. Therefore, it might fall under the opposite maxim: 'whoever delays something after its time is punished by deprivation.' But the ruling only states non-permissibility, not deprivation.
This shows that application of maxims requires careful identification of the operative legal cause (fi'l al-must'ajal).
5
Synthesize the correct application
To apply the maxim correctly: (1) identify the benefit sought, (2) identify the premature action, (3) determine if the action was specifically to secure that benefit early, (4) if so, the benefit is forfeited.
The killer's case satisfies all steps; the mukatib's case may not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة عشر : مَن استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه ومن فروعها؛ حرمان القاتل مورثه عن الإرث. ومنها ما ذكره الطحاوي في مشكل الآثار أن المكاتب إذا كان له قدرة على الأداء فأخره ليدوم له النظر إلى سيدته لم يجز له ذلك، لأنه منع واجبًا عليه ليبقى ما يحرم عليه إذا أذاه نقله عن السبكي في شرح المنهاج وقال إنه تخريج حسن لا يبعد من جهة الفقه . (اهـ ) . ولم يظهر لي كونها من فروعها وإنما هي من فروع ضدّها، وهو أنه من أخر لشيء بعد أوانه، فليتأمل في الحكم فإنه لم يذكر إلا عدم الجواز، فلم يعاقب بحرمان شيء .
The fifteenth maxim: Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it. Among its applications: The deprivation of a killer from inheriting from his victim. And among them is what al-Tahawi mentioned in Mushkil al-Athar: That a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission), if he has the ability to pay but delays in order for the look at his mistress to continue, that is not permissible for him, because he prevents what is obligatory upon him so that what is forbidden to him remains; he transmitted it from al-Subki in Sharh al-Minhaj and said that it is a good derivation not far from fiqh. (End). And it did not become clear to me that this is among its applications; rather it is among the applications of its opposite, namely that he who delays something after its time... so one should reflect on the ruling, for he only mentioned non-permissibility, not punishment by deprivation of something.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This is the fifteenth maxim: one who rushes something before its proper time is punished by losing it. The classic example is that a murderer is deprived of inheritance from his victim. Another application, from al-Tahawi, concerns a mukatib (a slave in a contract of manumission) who has the ability to pay but deliberately delays payment so that he can continue looking at his mistress (which he would be prohibited from after freedom). This is not permissible because he is preventing an obligatory payment to prolong a forbidden act. The author, however, questions whether this is truly an application of the maxim; he suggests it might be an application of the opposite maxim (delaying something after its time). He leaves it for further reflection.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has a contract with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a confirmed application of the maxim 'whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it'?
A) A mukatib delaying payment to continue looking at his mistress
B) A killer who murders his victim to inherit
C) A person who delays paying zakat
D) A person who prays before the time

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it' is a fundamental legal principle in Hanafi fiqh, applied primarily to cases like the killer's deprivation of inheritance, but its application requires careful analysis of whether the act is truly hastening or delaying.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Special Guardianship is Stronger than General Guardianship
Defining the maxim and applying it through furuat and a control (dabt).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' to identify the valid guardian in marriage, qisas, and waqf, and evaluate the hierarchy of guardians in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Special guardians (father, grandfather) have inherent authority that cannot be overridden by a qadi or imam.

This lesson explores the sixteenth maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, which establishes the hierarchy of guardianship. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when determining who has the right to contract marriage, execute qisas, or manage waqf property. We will examine the maxim's applications, the classification of guardians, and a key intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of guardianship
Distinguish between special guardianship (wilaya khassa) held by specific individuals like father, grandfather, and manumitter, and general guardianship (wilaya 'amma) held by the qadi or imam.
Special guardianship is inherent and cannot be removed, unlike agency which is revocable.
2
Identify the first application: marriage of orphans
The qadi cannot marry off an orphan boy or girl when a special guardian (father, grandfather, manumitter) exists. The special guardian's right takes precedence.
This is a clear practical consequence of the maxim.
3
Identify the second application: qisas and pardon
The special guardian has the right to execute qisas, make reconciliation, and pardon, while the imam (general guardian) cannot pardon.
The exception: the father of an insane person may execute qisas and reconcile but cannot pardon if the victim is his child's guardian (e.g., the father's own son is killed by his ward).
4
Understand the classification of guardians (dabt)
Guardians are categorized by scope: over both property and marriage (father/grandfather), over marriage only (other asaba, mother, dhawi al-arham), and over property only (executor). They are ranked: (1) father/grandfather (intrinsic), (2) agent (revocable), (3) executor (irrevocable by self), (4) waqf supervisor.
The father and grandfather's guardianship is an intrinsic attribute (صفة ذاتية).
5
Analyze the dispute over dismissal of waqf supervisors
The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed: Abu Yusuf allowed the founder to dismiss the supervisor without condition; Muhammad prohibited it. The later relied-upon opinion is Abu Yusuf's, especially in awqaf.
The qadi cannot dismiss a waqf supervisor unless treachery is proven, and cannot dispose of waqf property while a supervisor exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة عشرة : الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة ولهذا قالوا إن القاضي لا يزوّج اليتيم واليتيمة إلا عند عدم ولي لهما في النكاح أما أو معتقاً. وللولي الخاص استيفاء القصاص والصلح والعفو ولو ذا محرم أو رحم مجانا والإمام لا يملك العفو ولا يعارضه ما قال في الكنز : ولأب المعتوه القود والصلح لا العفو بقتل وليه لأنه فيما إذا قتل ولي المعتوه كابنه. قال في الكنز : والقاضي كالأب والوصي يصالح فقط أي فلا يقتل ولا يعفو . ضابط : الولي قد يكون وليا في المال والنكاح وهو الأب والجد، وقد يكون وليا في النكاح فقط وهو سائر العصبات والأم وذوو الأرحام، وقد يكون في المال فقط وهو الوصي الأجنبي. وظاهر كلام المشايخ أنها مراتب: الأولى ولاية الأب والجد وهي وصف ذاتي لهما. ونقل ابن السبكي الإجماع على أنهما لو عزلا أنفسهما لم ينعزلا . الثانية : السفلى ؛ وهي ولاية الوكيل ؛ وهي غير لازمة فللموكل عزله إن علم، وللوكيل عزل نفسه بعلم موكله . الثالثة : الوصية وهي بينهما فلم يجز له أن يعزل نفسه. الرابعة : ناظر الوقف. واختلف الشيخان فجوز الثاني للواقف عزلاً بلا اشتراط، ومنعه الثالث، واختلف التصحيح والمعتمد في الأوقاف والقضاء قول الثاني. وأما إذا عزل نفسه فإن أخرجه القاضي خرج كما في القنية وفي القنية : لا يملك القاضي التصرّف في مال اليتيم مع وجود وصية ولو كان منصوبه :(اهـ). وفي فتاوى رشيد الدين أن القاضي لا يملك عزل القيم على الوقف إلا عند ظهور الخيانة منه. وعلى هذا لا يملك القاضي التصرّف في الوقف مع وجود ناظر ولو من قبله . (اهـ).
The sixteenth maxim: Special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship. For this reason, they said that the qadi does not marry off the orphan boy or girl except in the absence of a guardian for them in marriage, such as a father or a manumitter. And the special guardian has the right to execute qisas, reconciliation, and pardon, even if there is a mahram or a relative for free, while the imam does not have the right to pardon, and it is not contradicted by what is said in al-Kanz: 'For the father of an insane person are qisas and reconciliation, not pardon for killing his guardian' because that is when the guardian of the insane person (like his son) is killed. Al-Kanz says: 'The qadi is like the father and the executor: they may only reconcile,' meaning they may not execute qisas or pardon. Control: The guardian may be a guardian over property and marriage, which is the father and grandfather; or over marriage only, which is other asabas, the mother, and dhawi al-arham; or over property only, which is the foreign executor. And the apparent meaning of the mashayikh's statements is that they are ranks: First: guardianship of the father and grandfather, which is an intrinsic attribute for them. Ibn al-Subki transmitted consensus that if they remove themselves, they are not removed. Second: the lower: the guardianship of the agent; it is not binding, so the principal may dismiss him if he knows, and the agent may dismiss himself with the knowledge of the principal. Third: executorship (al-wasiyya) which is between them, so he may not dismiss himself. Fourth: the supervisor of a waqf. The two shaykhs disagreed: the second permitted the waqf founder to dismiss without condition, and the third prohibited it. The correct and relied-upon opinion in awqaf and judiciary is the second's. As for if he dismisses himself, if the qadi expels him, he leaves, as in al-Qunyah. And in al-Qunyah: 'The qadi does not have the authority to dispose of the orphan's property when there is an executor, even if the executor was appointed by him.' (end). And in Fatawa Rashid al-Din: 'The qadi does not have the authority to dismiss the supervisor of a waqf except upon appearance of treachery from him.' Based on this, the qadi may not dispose of the waqf property while there is a supervisor, even if appointed by him. (end).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth maxim of al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: 'Special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship.' It begins by explaining the practical consequence: a qadi (general guardian) only marries off orphans when there is no special guardian (like father, grandfather, or manumitter). The special guardian has broader powers in qisas and pardon. The text then provides a detailed control (dabt) categorizing guardians by the scope of their authority (property, marriage, or both) and ranks them hierarchically from highest (father/grandfather) to lowest (waqf supervisor). It discusses disagreements about dismissal powers and concludes with the relied-upon rulings from later authorities like al-Qunyah and Rashid al-Din. The key point is that the special guardian's authority is inherent and cannot be removed by the qadi, while general guardians (agents, executors) are subject to dismissal under certain conditions.

Key Terms
ولاية خاصة (wilaya khassa): Guardianship over a specific person or matter by a designated individual (like father, grandfather).
ولاية عامة (wilaya 'amma): General guardianship held by a qadi or imam over persons without a specific guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, who has the authority to pardon qisas?
A) The imam (general guardian)
B) The qadi
C) The special guardian (father or heir)
D) The waqf supervisor

Key Takeaway
Special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship, so the qadi's authority is limited to cases where no special guardian exists.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Qaida 17: Manifestly Erroneous Conjecture is Disregarded
Stating the maxim and providing furuat with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه' to distinct fiqh scenarios, distinguish its application from its exceptions, and identify the mufta bihi position in the zakat dispute.

Core Idea
A clearly wrong assumption has no legal effect; the actual reality governs.

This lesson examines the seventeenth Hanafi legal maxim: 'No consideration is given to manifestly erroneous conjecture.' We will explore its meaning through concrete examples from prayer, wudu, and zakat, and then analyse the exceptions that appear to contradict it. Finally, we will resolve an intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat where the maxim is central.

Guided Path
1
Define the maxim and its scope
Understand what constitutes 'manifestly erroneous conjecture' – a belief that is clearly contradicted by reality. The maxim states that such conjecture carries no legal weight; the actual fact prevails.
The Arabic 'بين خطوه' means 'its error is evident'.
2
Work through the main examples
Analyse the three cases: praying Fajr thinking time is narrow, performing wudu with water thought to be impure, and giving zakat to a person thought to be disqualified. In each, the mistaken belief is ignored because it is manifestly wrong.
The first two cases show that the act is evaluated based on reality, not the false assumption.
3
Identify the exceptions (furuat)
The text lists four cases where the ruling differs. Examine each: giving zakat to someone thought to be a valid recipient when he is actually rich or one's son (partly valid by agreement), praying in a garment thought to be impure (repeat), praying thinking one is in hadath (repeat), and praying before the time thinking it had entered (repeat). Explain why these are not true exceptions but applications of other principles.
Exceptions arise when the mistaken belief is not 'manifestly erroneous' or when an explicit prohibition overrides the maxim.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute
In the zakat case where the recipient turns out to be rich or one's son, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the zakat suffices, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Apply the maxim to side with the majority: since the error was manifest, the actual status (wealth) should be ignored.
The text says 'عندهما اتفاقا' indicating the majority view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السابعة عشرة : لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه صرح به أصحابنا في مواضع منها في باب قضاء الفوائت قالوا: لو ظن أن وقت الفجر ضاق فصلى الفجر ثم تبين أنه كان في الوقت سعة بطل الفجر؛ فإذا بطل ينظر، فإن كان في الوقت سعة يصلِّي العشاء ثم يُعيد الفجر، فإن لم يكن فيه سعة يُعيد الفجر فقط. وتمامه في شرح الزيلعي . ومنها لو ظن الماء نجسًا فتوضأ به ثم تبين أنه طاهر جاز وضوءه، كذا في الخلاصة . ومنها لو ظن المدفوع إليه غير مصرف للزكاة فدفع له ثم تبين أنه مصرف أجزأه اتفاقا . وخرجت عن هذه القاعدة مسائل: الأولى : لو ظنه مصرفًا للزكاة فدفع له ثم تبيَّن أنه غني أو ابنه أجزأه عندهما اتفاقا . خلافا لأبي يوسف، وتبين أنه عبده أو مكاتبه أو حربي لم يجزه الثانية : لو صلّى في ثوب وعنده أنه نجس فظهر أنه طاهر أعاد . الثالثة : لو صلّى وعنده أنه محدث ثم ظهر أنه متوضىء. الرابعة : صلَّى الفرض وعنده أن الوقت لم يدخل فظهر أنه كان قد دخل لم يجزه فيهما، وهي في فتح القدير من الصلاة والثالثة تقتضي أن تحمل مسألة
The seventeenth maxim: No consideration is given to manifestly erroneous conjecture. Our companions explicitly stated this in several places, including the chapter on making up missed prayers. They said: if he thought that the time for Fajr was narrow [i.e., about to end] and he prayed Fajr, then it became clear that the time was ample, the Fajr prayer is invalid; then if it is invalid, we look: if there is ample time, he prays Isha and then repeats Fajr, but if there is no ample time, he repeats only Fajr. The full discussion is in Sharh al-Zayla'i. And among them: if he thought the water was impure and performed wudu with it, then it became clear that it was pure, his wudu is valid, as in al-Khulasa. And among them: if he thought the recipient was not a valid recipient of zakat and gave to him, then it became clear that he was a valid recipient, it suffices by agreement. And derived from this maxim are issues: First: if he thought he was a valid recipient and gave to him, then it became clear that he was rich or his son, it suffices by agreement of the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?] contrary to Abu Yusuf; and if it becomes clear that he was his slave, or his mukatab, or a harbi, it does not suffice. Second: if he prayed in a garment thinking it was impure, then it appeared pure, he repeats. Third: if he prayed thinking he was in a state of hadath [ritual impurity], then it appeared that he was in wudu. Fourth: he prayed an obligatory prayer thinking that the time had not entered, then it appeared that it had entered, it does not suffice in both cases, and it is in Fath al-Qadir from the prayer section, and the third requires that the issue be interpreted as...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth maxim: 'No consideration is given to manifestly erroneous conjecture.' The maxim means that if someone makes a judgment based on a clearly wrong assumption, that assumption has no legal effect. The text gives several examples: first, from missed prayers, if someone incorrectly thinks Fajr time is about to end and prays immediately, then finds there was ample time, the prayer is invalid (because the assumption was clearly wrong). The remedy depends on remaining time. Second, if one assumes water is impure but it is pure, wudu is valid (the assumption was erroneous but the reality was correct). Third, if one wrongly assumes a zakat recipient is disqualified but they are qualified, it suffices. Then the text lists exceptions (furuat that seem to contradict the maxim): cases where the erroneous assumption actually does not invalidate the act. These exceptions require careful analysis. The passage ends abruptly while discussing the fourth exception. The key is that the maxim applies only when the conjecture is 'manifestly erroneous' (بين خطوه), meaning the person had no reasonable basis for the assumption.

Key Terms
ظن بين خطوه (zann bayyin khattihi): A conjecture that is clearly and evidently wrong.
قضاء الفوائت (qada' al-fawa'it): Making up missed prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if someone prays Fajr thinking the time is about to end but finds ample time, what is the ruling on that prayer?
A) It is valid because the intention was correct.
B) It is invalid and must be repeated.
C) It is valid and no repetition is needed.
D) It depends on whether the time was truly narrow.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

No Regard to Manifestly Mistaken Conjecture: Maxim and Exceptions
Stating the maxim with numerous furuat and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه' to novel cases and identify when the maxim is set aside by its exceptions.

Core Idea
The Hanafi maxim invalidates acts based on manifestly wrong beliefs, but exceptions give effect to mistaken conjecture in specific cases.

This lesson unpacks the seventeenth major maxim of Hanafi fiqh: لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه. The maxim states that if a person acts based on a manifestly false presumption, the act is invalid. However, the Hanafi school identifies specific exceptions where the person's mistaken conjecture is given legal effect. Through numerous furuat (branches) from salah, zakat, taharah, and other chapters, you will learn to distinguish when a manifest error nullifies an act and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its scope
The maxim means that a conjecture which is clearly wrong (الظن البين خطوه) is not considered legally. The default ruling is that actions based on such mistaken beliefs are invalid.
The maxim applies across many fiqh chapters including salah, zakat, taharah, fasting, marriage, hudud, and divorce.
2
Apply the maxim to standard cases
Work through the examples: praying Fajr thinking time is short but it is ample (prayer invalid); performing wudu with water thought impure but it is pure (wudu valid); paying zakat to someone thought ineligible but they are eligible (zakat suffices). In each, actual reality prevails over the wrong guess.
These are direct applications of the maxim.
3
Identify the exceptions (الخارجات عن القاعدة)
The text lists four exceptions where the opposite applies: i) paying zakat to someone thought eligible but they are rich/one's son – it suffices (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad); ii) praying in a garment thought impure but it is pure – repeat; iii) praying thinking one is impure but is pure – repeat; iv) praying thinking time hasn't entered but it has – repeat.
In these, the mistaken belief (that there was an impediment) actually invalidates the act.
4
Analyse the rationale behind the exceptions
The key distinction: when the mistake concerns a condition for validity (like purity or time entry) and the person thought it was absent but it was present, the act is invalid (exception). When the mistake concerns an obstruction (like impurity of water) and the person thought it was present but it was absent, the act is valid (maxim applied).
The text summarises: 'ففي هذه المسائل الاعتبار لما ظنه المكلف لا لما في نفس الأمر، وعلى عكسه الاعتبار لما في نفس الأمر'.
5
Extend to other chapters
The passage applies the same principle to marriage (actual reality governs), hudud (blind man finds woman on bed – hadd applies), fasting (eating thinking it is night but it is day – qada only), Hajj (sick person's substitute if he recovers – must perform himself), debt (paying a thought debt – can take back).
Practice applying the maxim and its exceptions to these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السابعة عشرة : لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه
صرح به أصحابنا في مواضع منها في باب قضاء الفوائت قالوا: لو ظن أن وقت الفجر ضاق فصلى الفجر ثم تبين أنه كان في الوقت سعة بطل الفجر؛ فإذا بطل ينظر، فإن كان في الوقت سعة يصلِّي العشاء ثم يُعيد الفجر، فإن لم يكن فيه سعة يُعيد الفجر فقط. وتمامه في شرح الزيلعي . ومنها لو ظن الماء نجسًا فتوضأ به ثم تبين أنه طاهر جاز وضوءه، كذا في الخلاصة .
ومنها لو ظن المدفوع إليه غير مصرف للزكاة فدفع له ثم تبين أنه مصرف أجزأه اتفاقا . وخرجت عن هذه القاعدة مسائل:
الأولى : لو ظنه مصرفًا للزكاة فدفع له ثم تبيَّن أنه غني أو ابنه أجزأه عندهما اتفاقا . خلافا لأبي يوسف، و تبين أنه عبده أو مكاتبه أو حربي لم يجزه
الثانية : لو صلّى في ثوب وعنده أنه نجس فظهر أنه طاهر أعاد .
الثالثة : لو صلّى وعنده أنه محدث ثم ظهر أنه متوضىء.
الرابعة : صلَّى الفرض وعنده أن الوقت لم يدخل فظهر أنه كان قد دخل لم يجزه فيهما، وهي في فتح القدير من الصلاة والثالثة تقتضي أن تحمل مسألة الخلاصة سابقا على ما إذا لم يصل، أما إذا صلَّى فإنه يُعيد. ففي هذه المسائل الاعتبار لما ظنه المكلف لا لما في نفس الأمر، وعلى عكسه الاعتبار لما في نفس الأمر؛ فلو صلى وعنده أن الثوب طاهر أو أن الوقت قد دخل أو أنه متوضىء فبان خلافه أعاد. وينبغي أنه لو تزوج امرأة وعنده أنها غير محل فتبين أنها محل أو عكسه. أن يكون الاعتبار لما في نفس الأمر. وقالوا في الحدود: لو وطىء امرأة وجدها على فراشه ظانًا أنها امرأته فإنه يحدّ ولو كان أعمى. إلا إذا ناداها فأجابته. ولو أقر بطلاق زوجته ظانًا الوقوع بإفتاء المفتي فتبيَّن عدمه لم يقع كما في القنية. ولو أكل ظنه ليلاً فبانَ أنه بعد الطلوع قضى بلا تكفير ولو ظن الغروب فأكل ثم تبيَّن بقاء النهار قضى. وقالوا : لو رأوا سوادًا فظنوه عدوا فصلوا صلاة الخوف فبانَ خلافه لم تصح لأن الشرط حضور العدو وقالوا لو استناب المريض في حج ظانًا أنه لا يعيش ثم صح أداه بنفسه، ولو ظن أن عليه دينا فبان خلافه رجع بما أدّى، ولو خاطب امرأته بالطلاق ظانًا أنها أجنبية فبانَ أنها زوجته طلقت وكذا العتاق .
The seventeenth maxim: No regard is paid to manifestly mistaken conjecture. Our companions explicitly stated this in places, including the chapter on making up missed prayers. They said: If one thought that the time for Fajr was narrow and prayed Fajr, then it became clear that there was ample time, the Fajr is invalid. If it is invalid, then one looks: if there is ample time, one prays Isha and then repeats Fajr; if there is no ample time, one repeats only Fajr. The full details are in Sharh al-Zayla'i. Among them: If one thought water to be impure and performed wudu with it, then it became clear that it was pure, his wudu is valid, as in al-Khulasa. Among them: If one thought the recipient was not a recipient of zakat and gave to him, then it became clear that he was a recipient, it suffices by agreement. And there are exceptions to this maxim: The first: If one thought he was a recipient of zakat and gave to him, then it became clear that he was rich or his son, it suffices by agreement of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if it became clear that he was his slave, or his kitaba slave, or a harbi, it does not suffice. The second: If one prayed in a garment thinking it was impure, then it appeared to be pure, he must repeat. The third: If one prayed thinking he was in a state of hadath, then it appeared he was in wudu. The fourth: He prayed an obligatory prayer thinking the time had not entered, then it appeared that it had entered, it does not suffice in either case. These are in Fath al-Qadir from the chapter of prayer. And the third necessitates that the earlier issue from al-Khulasa be interpreted as when he did not pray; as for if he prayed, he must repeat. So in these issues, consideration is given to what the mukallaf thought, not to the actual reality. And on the opposite, consideration is given to actual reality: So if he prayed thinking the garment was pure, or that the time had entered, or that he was in wudu, and it turned out otherwise, he must repeat. And it should be that if he married a woman thinking she was not permissible (mahall), then it became clear she was permissible, or vice versa, the consideration is for actual reality. And they said in hudud: If a man had intercourse with a woman he found on his bed thinking she was his wife, he is subject to hadd, even if blind, unless he called her and she answered. And if he confessed to divorce of his wife thinking it occurred due to a mufti's fatwa, then it became clear it did not occur, it does not occur as in al-Qunyah. And if he ate thinking it was night, then it became clear it was after dawn, he makes qada without kaffarah; if he thought it was sunset and ate, then it became clear the day remained, he makes qada. And they said: If they saw a black mass and thought it was the enemy and prayed Salat al-Khawf, then it became clear otherwise, it is not valid because the condition is the presence of the enemy. And they said: If a sick person appointed someone for Hajj thinking he would not live, then recovered, he performs it himself. And if he thought he had a debt, then it became clear otherwise, he takes back what he paid. And if he addressed his wife with divorce thinking she was a foreign woman, then it turned out she was his wife, she is divorced, and likewise in emancipation.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth maxim: 'No regard is paid to manifestly mistaken conjecture.' It means that if a person acts based on a clearly wrong assumption, the act is invalid. The author then provides several applications from Hanafi sources. For example, if someone prays Fajr thinking time is short but finds there was ample time, the prayer is invalid and must be made up according to specific rules. Similarly, if one performs wudu with water he thought was impure but it is pure, the wudu is valid because the impurity was not real. Again, if someone gives zakat to a person he thought was not eligible but turns out to be eligible, it suffices. Then the passage lists four exceptions (مسائل خرجت عن القاعدة) where contrary to the maxim, consideration is given to the person's mistaken belief rather than reality. For instance, if he thought the recipient was eligible for zakat but he was actually rich or his son, it still suffices (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not Abu Yusuf). Also, if he prayed in a garment thinking it was impure but it was pure, he must repeat. The passage then clarifies the general principle: when the mistake involves a barrier (like impurity or lack of wudu), and he thought the barrier was present but it wasn't, his act is valid; when he thought the barrier was absent but it was present, his act is invalid. It also discusses related cases like marriage, hudud, fasting, and divorce, all illustrating the maxim and its exceptions.

Key Terms
الظن البين خطوه (al-ẓann al-bayyin khaṭa’uh): A conjecture that is clearly wrong (manifest error)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if a person performs wudu with water he thought was impure but it was pure, what is the ruling?
A) Wudu is invalid
B) Wudu is valid
C) He must repeat wudu
D) He must repeat prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'لا عبرة بالظن البين خطوه' invalidates acts based on manifestly wrong conjectures, but the school carves out important exceptions where the mistaken belief itself generates legal consequences.

Mentioning a Part of the Indivisible Is Like Mentioning the Whole
Stating the maxim, its furuat, and an exception with a dabt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله' to derive rulings in talaq, qisas, and hajj, identify the exception of manumission, and explain the dabt on zihar.

Core Idea
Mentioning part of an indivisible act has the same effect as the whole.

The Hanafi legal maxim 'ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله' governs cases where an act is legally indivisible. This lesson unpacks its furuat across divorce, retaliation, and pilgrimage, then examines the crucial exception of manumission and the unique controlling rule (dabt) where the part exceeds the whole.

Guided Path
1
Define legal indivisibility
Understand that 'ما لا يتجزأ' refers to acts whose legal effect cannot be split, such as divorce (talaq) and qisas. The maxim equates mentioning a part to the whole.
It is a legal, not physical, indivisibility.
2
Apply to talaq
If a husband says 'I divorce half of you,' the entire woman is divorced—the part triggers the whole ruling.
This also applies when saying 'one divorce' or half.
3
Apply to qisas
Pardoning part of the killer (e.g., a limb) constitutes full pardon; if some heirs pardon, the entire right to qisas falls and the remaining heirs receive monetary compensation.
4
Apply to hajj
Declaring 'I intend half of Hajj' results in full ihram for Hajj—the intention of part is intention for the whole.
The author notes he has not seen this explicitly, but it follows from the maxim.
5
Recognize the exception and dabt
Manumission is excluded per Abu Hanifa because it is divisible; freeing part does not free the whole. Additionally, a dabt states: the part may exceed the whole only in zihar—saying 'zahr' (back) makes it explicit, while 'umm' (mother) is kinayah.
The dabt restricts the maxim's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثامنة عشرة : ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله
فإذا طلق تطليقة وقعت واحدة أو طلق نصف المرأة طلقت .
ومنها العفو عن القصاص إذا عفى عن بعض القاتل كان عفوًا عن كله، وكذا إذا عفا بعض الأولياء سقط كله وانقلب نصيب الباقين مالاً.
ومنها النسك؛ إذ قال أحرمت بنصف نسك كان محرمًا، ولم أره الآن صريحًا. وخرج عن القاعدة العتق عند أبي حنيفة فإنه إذا أعتق بعض عبده لم يعتق كله، ولكن لم يدخل لأنه مما يتجزأ عنده والكلام فيما لا يتجزأ.
ضابط
لا يزيد البعض على الكل إلا في مسألة واحدة وهي إذا قال: أنت علي كظهر أمي فإنه صريح، ولو قال: كامي، كان كناية.
The eighteenth maxim: Mentioning a part of something indivisible is like mentioning the whole. Thus, if he divorced (saying) one divorce, it occurs as one; or if he divorced half of the woman, she is divorced. Among them is pardon from retaliation: if he pardoned part of the killer, it is a pardon of the whole; likewise, if some of the heirs pardon, the whole falls and the share of the remaining becomes monetary. Among them is the ritual: if he said 'I have entered ihram for half a Hajj', he is in ihram; but I have not now seen it explicitly. And an exception to the maxim is manumission according to Abu Hanifa: if he emancipates part of his slave, the whole does not become free, but this is not included because emancipation is divisible in his view, and the maxim pertains to what is indivisible. A controlling rule: A part does not exceed the whole except in one issue: if he says 'You are to me like the back of my mother', it is explicit; and if he says 'like my mother', it is a kinayah.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the eighteenth maxim: 'Mentioning a part of something indivisible is like mentioning the whole.' It means that for actions or objects that cannot be divided, specifying a part has the same legal effect as specifying the whole. The author provides applications: (1) Divorce: if one says 'I divorce you with one divorce' or 'I divorce half of you', the entire woman is divorced. (2) Retaliation (qisas): if the victim's family pardons part of the killer (e.g., one limb), it is considered pardon for the entire killing, and if some heirs pardon, the right to qisas falls and the shares of other heirs become monetary. (3) Hajj: if one says 'I intend half of Hajj', the whole Hajj is intended. The exception is manumission according to Imam Abu Hanifa: if one says 'I free half of my slave', the whole does not become free because Abu Hanifa considers manumission divisible; thus, it is outside the maxim. The passage also includes a dabt (controlling rule): 'A part does not exceed the whole except in one case' – that is, in the case of zihar (declaration of unlawful resemblance), if someone says 'You are to me like the back of my mother' (using the explicit word 'zahr'), it is explicit and triggers zihar; but if he says 'like my mother' (kahl), it is a kinayah (allusion) and does not automatically trigger zihar. This is an exception because the part ('zahr') has a stronger effect than the whole ('umm'), which is unusual.

Key Terms
ما لا يتجزأ (mā lā yatajazzā’): Something that is legally indivisible, like divorce or qisas
ضابط (ḍābiṭ): A controlling rule that restricts or provides an exception to the maxim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if a person says 'I divorce half of you', what is the effect?
A) Only half a divorce occurs
B) The entire woman is divorced
C) No divorce occurs
D) It is considered a kinayah

Key Takeaway
The maxim applies to legally indivisible acts; the key exception is manumission, and a unique dabt governs zihar.

Attributing Ruling to the Direct Agent (Al-Mubashir)
Stating the maxim with initial furuat on tort liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب أضيف الحكم إلى المباشر' to determine liability in tort cases, distinguishing between direct and indirect causes.

Core Idea
Liability falls on the direct actor, not the indirect cause.

This lesson introduces the nineteenth legal maxim from the Hanafi corpus: 'When the direct agent and the indirect cause come together, the ruling is attributed to the direct agent.' This maxim is fundamental in assigning liability in cases of harm (jinayat). Understanding it helps the mufti correctly attribute responsibility when both a direct perpetrator and an indirect enabler are involved.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Identify the two parties: al-mubashir (the one who directly causes the harm) and al-mutasabbib (the one who sets the stage indirectly).
These terms are central to causation analysis in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the maxim clearly
Recite the maxim: 'إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب أضيف الحكم إلى المباشر'. Understand that when both are present, the direct agent bears the ruling (e.g., liability).
Memorize the Arabic text as it is the foundation.
3
Analyze the rationale
The direct agent's act is the immediate cause of harm; the indirect cause is too remote. The ruling goes to the most proximate cause.
This aligns with the general principle in tort law that liability follows causation.
4
Apply to examples
Work through the two examples given: (1) well digger vs. thrower, (2) thief guide vs. thief. Confirm that the direct agent is liable.
Practice with other similar scenarios to solidify understanding.
5
Recognize exceptions
Note that if the direct agent is not legally responsible (e.g., a child, insane person, or animal), the indirect cause may be held liable. This is a key exception to the maxim.
This prevents the maxim from being applied mechanically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة التاسعة عشرة
إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب أضيف الحكم إلى المباشر، فلا ضمان على حافر البير تعديًا بما أتلف بإلقاء غيره ولا يضمن من دل سارقًا على مال إنسان فسرقه
The nineteenth maxim: When the direct agent and the indirect cause come together, the ruling is attributed to the direct agent. Thus, there is no liability on one who digs a well wrongfully for what is destroyed by the throwing of another, nor does one who guides a thief to a person's property guarantee it if he steals it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the nineteenth maxim: 'When the direct agent and the indirect cause come together, the ruling is attributed to the direct agent.' It means that if harm occurs through the actions of both a direct perpetrator and an indirect cause, liability falls solely on the direct agent. The author gives two examples: (1) If someone wrongfully digs a well, and another person throws something (or someone) into the well causing damage, the digger is not liable because the direct act of throwing caused the damage. (2) If someone points out a person's property to a thief, and the thief steals it, the pointer is not liable for the theft because the thief is the direct agent. This maxim is foundational in determining liability in torts (jinayat) and is applied across many chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubāshir): The direct agent who physically causes the harm
المتسبب (al-mutasabbib): The indirect cause who sets the stage for harm but does not directly perpetrate it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If A digs a well wrongfully, and B pushes C into the well, causing injury, who is liable?
A) A only
B) B only
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Liability in torts is attributed to the direct agent when both a direct and indirect cause are present, except when the direct agent lacks legal responsibility.

The Maxim: Mentioning Part of Indivisible Is Like Mentioning the Whole
States maxim and applies it to divorce, qisas, ihram, with exception of emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله' to new cases and identify its exception regarding slavery.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, mentioning part of an indivisible thing legally counts as the whole – except when the thing is divisible like slavery.

This lesson unpacks a key legal maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir that governs situations where only part of an indivisible entity is mentioned. Understanding when a part triggers the full ruling is essential for issuing correct fatwas in cases of partial divorce, partial pardon in qisas, or partial intention in ihram. The maxim also includes a notable exception that distinguishes slavery from other indivisible matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal object and its divisibility
First, determine whether the object of the legal act is indivisible (e.g., life, marriage bond, ihram) or divisible (e.g., slavery according to Abu Hanifa). Only if it is indivisible does the maxim apply.
The Hanafi school considers slavery divisible; others may differ.
2
Apply the maxim to the case
If the object is indivisible, then mentioning a part legally stands for mentioning the whole. For example, divorcing 'half a woman' results in a full divorce; pardoning 'part of a killer' pardons the entire qisas.
The maxim is a tool to avoid ambiguity in legal utterances.
3
Check for exceptions
The maxim has one exception (dabt): the part does not exceed the whole except in zihar. Also, if the object is divisible (like slavery), the maxim does not apply outright – only the mentioned part takes effect.
The zihar exception: saying 'like my mother' is kinayah, while 'like the back of my mother' is explicit.
4
Issue the fatwa
Combine the maxim and its exceptions to derive the correct ruling. For example, in a case where one guardian pardons part of the killer, the qisas falls for all, and the remaining guardians receive blood money.
The mufta bihi position is that of the maxim's general application, with Abu Hanifa's exception noted for slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثامنة عشرة : ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله فإذا طلق تطليقة وقعت واحدة أو طلق نصف المرأة طلقت . ومنها العفو عن القصاص إذا عفى عن بعض القاتل كان عفوًا عن كله، وكذا إذا عفا بعض الأولياء سقط كله وانقلب نصيب الباقين مالاً. ومنها النسك؛ إذ قال أحرمت بنصف نسك كان محرمًا، ولم أره الآن صريحًا. وخرج عن القاعدة العتق عند أبي حنيفة فإنه إذا أعتق بعض عبده لم يعتق كله، ولكن لم يدخل لأنه مما يتجزأ عنده والكلام فيما لا يتجزأ. ضابط لا يزيد البعض على الكل إلا في مسألة واحدة وهي إذا قال: أنت علي كظهر أمي فإنه صريح، ولو قال: كامي، كان كناية.
The eighteenth maxim: Mentioning part of that which is indivisible is like mentioning the whole. So if he divorces [with] one divorce, it occurs as one; or if he divorces half of a woman, she is divorced. And among its applications: pardon from qisas if he pardons part of the killer, it is a pardon for the whole; likewise if some of the guardians pardon, all [qisas] falls and the share of the remaining turns into property. And among them: the ritual, if he says 'I have entered ihram for half a ritual', he is in ihram, but I have not seen it explicitly. And excluded from the maxim: emancipation according to Abu Hanifa, for if he frees part of his slave, the whole is not freed, but it is not included because [slavery] is divisible according to him, and the discussion is about the indivisible. Rule: The part does not exceed the whole except in one case: if he says 'You are to me like the back of my mother', it is explicit; if he says 'like my mother', it is a kinayah.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the eighteenth maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, which applies to actions or objects that are legally indivisible (e.g., divorce, life, ihram). If one applies divorce to half a woman, the entire divorce takes effect because marital bond is indivisible. Similarly, pardoning part of a killer in qisas pardons the entire life; pardoning by some guardians extinguishes all qisas and converts the remaining share to blood money. For ihram, entering it for half a ritual means one is fully in ihram. An exception is made for manumission according to Abu Hanifa: freeing part of a slave does not free the whole because slavery is divisible in Hanafi fiqh. The 'dabt' (restrictive rule) adds that, under this maxim, a part cannot have a stronger effect than the whole, except in one case: in zihar, saying 'You are like the back of my mother to me' is explicit (zihar), while saying 'like my mother' is only a kinayah (allusion). This shows the nuance of legal language even within indivisible concepts.

Key Terms
ما لا يتجزأ (ma la yatajazza'u): Something legally indivisible, such as a person's life, marriage bond, or ihram.
العفو عن القصاص (al-'afw 'an al-qisas): Pardoning the right to retaliation in murder or injury, which is indivisible.
ضابط (dabit): A restrictive rule or boundary that limits the application of the maxim; here, the part does not exceed the whole except in one zihar case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if a person says 'I pardon half of the killer' in a qisas case, what is the ruling?
A) Only half of the qisas is forgiven.
B) The entire qisas is forgiven.
C) The pardon is invalid because it is partial.
D) The killer must still pay half the blood money.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله' is a powerful tool for resolving cases of partial legal acts, with the critical exception that it only applies to indivisible matters, and slavery is deemed divisible by Abu Hanifa.

The Maxim: Direct Cause Prevails Over Indirect Cause
States maxim and lists applications and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, apply it to at least three fiqh scenarios, and identify the exceptions where the indirect cause is liable.

Core Idea
Liability attaches to the direct actor unless the indirect cause breaches a duty or creates extreme risk.

This lesson unpacks a cornerstone Hanafi maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: when a direct cause (mubashir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib) meet, the ruling goes to the direct cause. But exceptions exist where the indirect cause is held liable due to dereliction of duty or stronger proximate link. We will examine the maxim, its core applications, and the nuanced exceptions that a mufti must know.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim
Read the maxim in Arabic and English. Identify the two actors: al-mubashir (direct cause) and al-mutasabbib (indirect cause). The literal meaning: when they combine, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause.
The maxim is wusta (intermediate) in rank, governing torts, hudud, and more.
2
Study the core applications (furuat)
Go through the five furuat given in the text: well-digger, directing a thief, directing to a fortress, falsely claiming a woman is free, giving a knife to a minor. For each, confirm that the direct cause bears liability/result, not the indirect cause.
These illustrate the general rule.
3
Analyze the exceptions (istithna'at)
Six exceptions are listed: depositary, guardian/agent, muhrim directing to game, collector (sa'i), giving knife causing direct injury. Understand why each exception flips liability: breach of trust, misrepresentation, removal of security, custom, direct injury.
Exceptions prove the rule; without them the maxim would be overbroad.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text contrasts the view of Imam Muhammad (liability on indirect cause in certain cases) with the Sahibayn (no liability). Note the practical difference and which view is mufta bihi (later fatwa leans toward Sahibayn).
This shows that even within a school, nuanced ikhtilaf exists.
5
Apply the maxim in a fatwa context
Practice applying the maxim to a new scenario: e.g., a person hires a worker who negligently causes damage. Distinguish between direct vs indirect causation and decide liability.
The mufti must assess causal proximity and any duty breach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة التاسعة عشرة إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب أضيف الحكم إلى المباشر، فلا ضمان على حافر البير تعديًا بما أتلف بإلقاء غيره ولا يضمن من دل سارقًا على مال إنسان فسرقه، ولا سهم لمن دلَّ على حصن دار الحرب ولا ضمان على من قال تزوجها فإنها حرَّة، فظهر بعد الولادة أنها أمة ولا ضمان على من دفع إلى صبي سكينا أو سلاحًا ليمسكه فقتل به نفسه وخرجت عنها مسائل : الولد. منها : لو دل المودع السارق على الوديعة فإنه يضمن لترك الحفظ. الثانية : لو قال ولي المرأة تزوّجها فإنها حرَّة. الثالثة : قال وكيلها ذلك فولدت ثم ظهر أنها أمة الغير، رجع المغرور بقيمة الولد. الرابعة : دل محرم حلالاً على صيد فقتله وجب الجزاء على الدال بشرطه في محله لإزالة الأمن بخلاف الدلالة على صيد الحرم، فإنها لا توجب شيئًا لبقاء أمنه بالمكان بعدها. فائدة. الخامسة : الإفتاء بتضمين الساعي، وهو قول المتأخرين لغلبة السعاية. السادسة لو دفع إلى صبي سكينًا ليمسكه له فوقعت عليه فجرحته كان على الدافع. في حفر البئر ؛ قال الولي : سقط. وقال الحافر : أسقط نفسه. فالقول للحافر، كذا في التوضيح. تکمیل يُضاف الحكم إلى حفر البئر وشق الزق وقطع حبل القنديل وفتح باب القفص على قول محمد وعندهما لا ضمان كحل قيد العبد وتمامه في شرحنا على المنار، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
The nineteenth maxim: When the direct and indirect causes combine, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause. So there is no liability on the digger of a well for trespass for damage caused by someone else throwing [something]; and the one who directs a thief to someone's property is not liable if the thief steals; and no share for one who points to a fortress of the enemy; and no liability on one who says 'marry her, she is free', and it becomes clear after birth that she is a slave; and no liability on one who gives a knife or weapon to a minor to hold, and he kills himself with it. And excluded from it are cases: the child. Among them: if the depositary directs the thief to the deposit, he is liable for abandoning custody. Second: if the guardian of a woman says 'marry her, she is free'. Third: if her agent says that, and she gives birth then it becomes clear she is someone else's slave, the deceived party recovers the value of the child. Fourth: if a muhrim directs a halal person to game and he kills it, the penalty is upon the director under its conditions at his place because security is removed, unlike directing to game in the Haram, which does not oblige anything because the security of the place remains after it. Benefit: Fifth: the fatwa to make the collector liable, which is the view of the later scholars due to the prevalence of collection. Sixth: if he gives a knife to a minor to hold and it falls and injures him, liability is on the giver. Regarding digging the well: the guardian says: he fell; and the digger says: he threw himself. So the statement is of the digger, as in al-Tawdih. Completion: The ruling is attributed to the well-digger, splitting the waterskin, cutting the lamp's rope, and opening the door of the cage according to Muhammad; according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], there is no liability like loosening the fetter of a slave, and its completion is in our commentary on al-Manar. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the nineteenth maxim, which establishes that when a direct cause (mubashir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib) are both present, legal liability is attributed to the direct cause. For example, a person who digs a well on another's property is not liable if someone else throws an object into it causing damage—the thrower is the direct cause. Similarly, a person who merely points out a thief's target is not liable for the theft; the thief is. However, there are six exceptions listed where the indirect cause is held liable due to a stronger connection: (1) a depositary who directs a thief to the deposit forfeits his custodial role and is liable; (2) a guardian who falsely claims a woman is free; (3) an agent who does the same; (4) a muhrim who directs a non-muhrim to game in the Haram—he is liable for the penalty (jaza') because he removed the safety; (5) a later fatwa makes the collector (sa'i) liable due to his aggressive collection; (6) giving a knife to a minor results in liability for injuries. The 'Tatmim' (completion) adds that according to Imam Muhammad, digging a well, splitting a water-skin, cutting a lamp rope, and opening a cage all attribute liability to the digger/splitter/cutter/opener, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), there is no liability, likening it to loosening a slave's fetter. The author refers to his commentary on al-Manar for more detail.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubashir): The direct cause that immediately brings about the effect (e.g., the thrower, the killer).
المتسبب (al-mutasabbib): The indirect cause that creates the conditions for the effect (e.g., the well-digger, the guide).
الضمان (al-daman): Liability, guarantee, or obligation to compensate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, who is liable if a person digs a well on someone's land and another person accidentally falls into it?
A) The digger is liable because he started the chain.
B) The person who fell is liable unless he was pushed.
C) No one is liable because it was an accident.
D) The landowner is liable.

Key Takeaway
The maxim directs liability to the direct cause, but a mufti must carefully check whether the indirect cause belongs to one of the six exceptions where liability attaches due to duty or proximity.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Liability Multiplies with Multiple Actors, Not for Sanctuary
A legal maxim with its furu' and istithna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'daman al-fi'l yata'addadu bi ta'addudi al-fa'il' to distinguish between multiple penalties for muhrims and a single penalty for sanctuary violation.

Core Idea
Liability for acts in ihram multiplies with actors; violation of sanctuary alone does not.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi legal maxim from the Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir tradition. The maxim distinguishes between two types of liability: one that multiplies with the number of perpetrators (acts during ihram) and one that remains singular (violation of the Haram's sanctity). Understanding this distinction is crucial for issuing correct fatwas on hunting penalties during Hajj.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Read the maxim in Arabic and English. The maxim has two parts: 'daman al-fi'l yata'addadu bi ta'addudi al-fa'il' (liability for an act multiplies with the multiplicity of actors) and 'wa daman al-hill la' (liability for violating the sanctuary does not).
2
Identify the furu' (application)
The author gives the example: two muhrims jointly kill game. Apply the first part of the maxim: each must pay a separate penalty (jaza'). This is because the violation is of the state of ihram, which is a right of God that can be multiplied.
The penalty is determined by the value of the game animal.
3
Identify the istithna' (exception)
The maxim's second part provides an exception: if non-muhrims (hull) kill game in the Haram, the penalty does not multiply. This is because the violation is of the sanctity of the place, not the state of ihram, and it is treated like a right of God that is singular.
The author analogizes this to 'rights of slaves' (huquq al-'ibad), which are not multiplied.
4
Relate to underlying principle
The distinction hinges on the nature of the violated right. Multiplicity applies to states (ihram) that can be repeated; singularity applies to fixed sanctities (Haram). This aligns with the broader Hanafi principle that daman in huquq Allah can be pluralized, but not in huquq al-'ibad.
This maxim refines the general rule by introducing an exception based on the locus of violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمان الفعل يتعدد بتعدد الفاعل وضمان الحلّ لا . فلو اشترك محرمان في قتل صيد تعدّد الجزاء، ولو حلاً لأن في قتل صيد الحرم لا كضمان حقوق العباد.
Liability for an act multiplies with the multiplicity of the actor, and liability for violating the sanctuary does not. So if two muhrims (in ihram) jointly kill game, the penalty is multiplied; but if (they kill) in the state of being non-muhrim (hull) in killing game in the Haram, it is not like liability for the rights of slaves.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim from Hanafi fiqh: liability for a prohibited act multiplies with the number of actors when the act is a tort against God's rights (like killing game in ihram). However, if the violation is of the sanctuary (Haram) without ihram, the penalty is not multiplied, as it is akin to violating God's sanctity, not slave's rights. The application: two muhrims jointly killing game must each pay a separate penalty (fidyah), but if two non-muhrims kill game in the Haram, only one penalty is due.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Legal liability or responsibility to make amends (penalty/compensation).
جزاء (jaza'): Penalty for killing game in ihram, typically a sacrifice or equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two individuals in ihram jointly hunt and kill a deer. How many penalties are due according to the maxim?
A) One penalty total.
B) Two penalties, one each.
C) Four penalties.
D) No penalty because joint act.

Key Takeaway
The maxim refines liability by distinguishing between violations of state (ihram) which multiply, and violations of place (Haram) which do not.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Maxim on Shared Rights and Its Exceptions
A legal maxim with three exceptions and a qualifying rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'ma thabita li-jama'ah' and identify its three exceptions, applying the qualifying rule of indivisibility.

Core Idea
Rights of a group are shared unless indivisible, then each holds fully.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi qawida: whatever right is established for a group, it is shared proportionally—except when the right is indivisible. Three practical exceptions test this rule: marriage guardianship, inherited retaliation, and removal of public harm. You will learn both the maxim and the dabit that governs its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its default ruling
Identify the exact Arabic: 'ما ثبت لجماعة فهو بينهم على سبيل الاشتراك'. This means any right or property that belongs to a group is held proportionally (mushaa), not each having full independent right.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qawida in the Hanafi framework.
2
List the three exceptional cases
The text explicitly mentions three cases where the right vests in each individual on the basis of completeness (على سبيل الكمال): (1) guardianship of marriage for minors, (2) inherited retaliation, and (3) removal of general harm from a public road.
Memorize these three as they are frequently tested.
3
Understand the qualifying rule (dabt)
The dabit states: if the right is indivisible (لا يتجزيء), it vests in each fully; otherwise it is shared. This explains why the three exceptions are exceptions—they involve indivisible rights.
The dabit allows analogical extension to new cases beyond the listed exceptions.
4
Apply the dabit to the given example
The text gives the example of usage of a jointly owned slave (الاستخدام في المملوك مما يتجزى). Such usage is divisible (e.g., time-sharing), so it remains shared, not an exception.
This demonstrates the counter-case: when a right is divisible, the maxim applies.
5
Distinguish between shared and complete rights in practice
For each exception, articulate the practical consequence: e.g., any guardian can contract marriage; any heir can execute qisas; any passerby can demand removal of obstruction.
This is crucial for issuing fatwa correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ثبت لجماعة فهو بينهم على سبيل الاشتراك إلا في مسائل : الأولى : ولاية الإنكاح للصغير والصغيرة ثابتة للأولياء على سبيل الكمال الثانية: القصاص الموروث يثبت لكلِّ من الورثة على الكمال، حتى قال الإمام للوارث الكبير استيفاؤه قبل بلوغ الصغير بخلاف ما إذا كان البالغين، فإن الحاضر لا يملكه في غيبة الآخر اتفاقا، لاحتمال العفو. الثالثة : ولاية المطالبة بإزالة الضرر العام عن طريق المسلمين تثبت لكل من له حق المرور على الكمال والضابط أن التحق إذا كان مما لا يتجزيء فإنه يثبت لكل على الكمال فالاستخدام في المملوك مما يتجزى.
Whatever is established for a group is among them on the basis of sharing, except in the following matters: First: The guardianship of marriage for a minor boy and minor girl is established for the guardians on the basis of completeness. Second: Inherited retaliation is established for each of the heirs on the basis of completeness, such that Imam Abu Hanifa said the major heir may execute it before the minor reaches maturity, unlike the case when they are both adults, for the present one does not own it in the absence of the other by consensus, due to the possibility of pardon. Third: The guardianship of demanding removal of general harm from the path of Muslims is established for each person who has the right of passage on the basis of completeness. The rule is that if it is something that is not divisible, then it is established for each on the basis of completeness. Thus, usage of a slave owned in common is divisible.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This is a legal maxim (qawida) with its exceptions (furuat). The maxim states: 'Whatever is established for a group is among them on the basis of sharing' — meaning rights or goods that belong to multiple people are shared proportionally (mushaa), not each having full independent right. However, there are three exceptions where the right is established for each individual on the basis of completeness (istikmal), as if each has the full right. The first exception: guardianship of marriage (wilayah al-inkah) for minor children. The guardians (like father or grandfather) each have full authority to contract marriage for the minor; they do not share this power. The second exception: inherited retaliation (qisas) — each heir has the full right to demand and execute retaliation. Imam Abu Hanifa even rules that a major heir can execute it before a minor heir reaches maturity. But if all heirs are adults, the one present cannot execute it in the absence of another, due to the possibility of pardon. The third exception: guardianship of removal of general harm (like encroachment on a public road) — each person with right of passage has full authority to demand its removal. Then the text gives a qualifying rule (dabit): if the right is indivisible (la yatajazzau), then it vests in each fully. Conversely, if divisible, it remains shared. The example: usage of a jointly owned slave is divisible (each can use the slave part of the time), so it does not fall under the exception.

Key Terms
على سبيل الاشتراك (ala sababil ishtirak): On a shared basis, meaning the right is divided among the group proportionally.
على سبيل الكمال (ala sababil kamal): On a complete basis, meaning each individual holds the full right independently.
لا يتجزيء (la yatajazzau): Indivisible; the right cannot be split up among individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an exception to the maxim that rights established for a group are shared?
A) Shared ownership of a house
B) Guardianship of marriage for minors
C) Joint debt owed to the group
D) Common grazing land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qawida of shared rights is qualified by indivisibility: indivisible rights vest fully in each holder.
